Date and signature 
==PAGE:3==
ii 
 
ABSTRACT 
Renovation plays a key role in achieving the 2050 carbon-neutral goals for the EU construction 
sector. Several decision support tools are available to find the most sustainable renovation alternative. 
The considerations about demolition and replacement of an existing building with a new one in these 
tools and renovation design process in general are very limited. This research proposes and shows a 
methodology how to include this scenario as one of the solution alternatives for sustainable 
renovation decision-making. An imaginary scenario of a new building replacing the old one after its 
demolition is introduced, where the new building meets the requirements of current standards for 
new buildings while keeping fixed as many parameters, as they are in the best renovation alternative, 
as possible. In a case study, this scenario is compared with a real case of renovation and an 
additionally introduced scenario of a replacing building, having the same parameters as the 
renovation case. Environmental impact, life cycle cost and indoor environment quality of all three 
scenarios are evaluated over a 30, 50 and 100-year study period. Sustainability of these solutions is 
finally evaluated within a 50-year study period with one of the already existing sustainability 
evaluation methods.   
The analysis pointed out the large influence of the energy performance in both the environmental 
and economic impact. The lower the consumption is, the higher the influence of the building material 
is. If a renovation brings only small improvements in the energy performance of the existing building, 
it can be expected that the demolition and replacement with a new building would bring a more 
sustainable solution, if considering the whole building life cycle. It is recommended to continue in 
the research to confirm the obtained results in other types of buildings. Several observations, 
obstacles and ideas for improvements of the sustainable renovation design are included as well. This 
research showed that the traditional mindset about sustainable renovation might not be correct and 
needs alterations.

iii 
 
PREFACE AND ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS 
This master thesis was carried out as a partial fulfillment of the requirements for the Master of 
Science in Architectural Engineering, study line Energy design at the Technical University of 
Denmark, DTU.  The thesis titled ‘Evaluation of deep renovation of an existing mixed-use building 
versus its demolition and replacement with a new building’ is a result of a 5-month research, which 
was extended with 6 weeks due to two special courses taken at DTU alongside the master thesis 
research. One of the courses called Life Cycle Management of Buildings, supervised by Per Anker 
Jensen, provided the background necessary for conducting this research. The final report 
summarizing the knowledge gained in this course is included as Appendix Z, as it provides more 
detailed explanation of the methodology used in the research. It should not be part of the final 
evaluation of this project because it was already graded separately.  
The research was conducted at DTU Civil Engineering (DTU BYG) and DTU Management 
Engineering (DTU MAN) in cooperation with a consultant engineering company NIRAS. The 
methodology suggested within this project was presented in a case study and the company NIRAS 
provided data for this case. Therefore, I would like to express my gratitude to everyone at NIRAS, 
especially to Maja Sofie Heiede Christensen who was assisting me with questions about the case and 
Peter Noyé who agreed with the company cooperation with this research. Special thanks go to my 
supervisors, Søren Peter Bjarløv at DTU BYG and Per Anker Jensen at DTU MAN for their 
continuous guidance, consultations and help. Furthermore, I would like to thank to Morten Birkved 
and Susanne Balslev Nielsen from DTU MAN for their assistance with Life Cycle Assessment. Last, 
but not least, I would like to thank to my friends and family for their infinite support which motivated 
me throughout my whole studies.

1 
 
1 INTRODUCTION 
The building sector counts for 40% of the total energy consumption in Europe.1 The renovation is 
considered to play a key role in achieving the 2050 decarbonization goals for the EU construction 
sector.2 Circumscribing the situation to Danish conditions, the aim of the Danish government is that 
the fossil fuels should be eliminated for space heating in buildings by 2035 and completely by 2050. 
In order to reach this goal, it was estimated that the energy consumption of the existing building 
stock should be reduced by about 60-70%.3 
The requirements for the energy performance of renovation projects are lower than for new 
buildings,4 partially due to limitations caused by the already existing construction in the renovation 
case.5 Historically, about 1% of the Danish building stock is replaced every year.3 Is it more 
sustainable to renovate a building or demolish it and replace it with a new one? Or rather, when is it 
more sustainable to renovate a building than tear it down and replace it? These questions were the 
impulse for conducting the research presented in this report. 
The idea was to create a decision support tool for sustainable renovation decision-making considering 
the demolition and replacement of an existing building as one of the solution alternatives. This 
decision support tool should compare the most sustainable retrofit solution (typically a deep 
renovation meaning large energy savings) with a new imaginary scenario. In this scenario, an existing 
building is demolished and replaced with a new building, which meets the current requirements for 
new buildings while keeping fixed as many parameters, as they are in the best renovation alternative, 
as possible.  
The research started with a state of the art of the sustainable renovation decision-making and the 
decision support tools, which is presented at the beginning of the report. Based on this review, the 
research methodology is described where the different alternatives are compared in terms of three 
sustainability pillars. The environmental impact is assessed by a method called life cycle assessment. 
The economic efficiency is evaluated through life cycle cost. The social pillar is mainly evaluated 
with regards to the indoor environment quality.   
The methodology is afterwards applied to a case study. A real case of deep renovation serves as a 
reference case and is compared to two other scenarios. An extra scenario is introduced in this case 
study in order to investigate the processes’ differences. This scenario 2 represents a demolition of 
the existing building and its replacement with a new building, which has the same parameters as the 
reference case. Finally, the third scenario considers the demolition of the existing building and its 
replacement with a new building – meeting the current requirements for new buildings as described 
earlier.  
Because it is important to assess a product through its whole life cycle in the sustainability 
assessment, the three scenarios were investigated and compared within 30, 50 and 100-year study 
period. As a part of the decision support tool, the scenarios were compared within a 50-year time 
span, which is a recommended study period in DGNB methodology, widely used in sustainability 
assessment approach. 
At the end of this report, the obstacles, observations and ideas for improvements in the proposed 
methodology, sustainable renovation decision-making and sustainable renovation in general are 
discussed. 
==PAGE:11==
2 
 
2 STATE OF THE ART

building retrofit program are described.6

The energy situation has forced the decision makers to examine the resulting impact of various 
courses of action in greater detail.7 The sustainable approach in renovation process means 
consideration of social (SOC) and economic (ECO) development and environmental (ENV) 
protection from the whole life cycle point of view. The environmental and economic analyses do not 
necessarily go hand in hand.8 Several tools have been developed to assist the stakeholders in 
sustainable decision-making in different stages of the renovation process in order to assess the three 
pillars and contribute to sustainable development as can be seen in Figure 1. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 1 Three pillars of sustainable development9 
As the main idea of this research was to create a decision support tool for sustainable renovation 
including the demolition and replacement as one of the solution alternatives within the design 
process, the literature review on the current state of the art regarding the tools was conducted. It was 
found that large amount of research, methods and tools had been focused on the sustainable 
renovation. It was also found that the discussions and considerations of demolition and replacement 
of an existing building are very limited.  
Decision-making in the building design process consists of two essential parts – determination of 
solution alternatives and criteria assessment on the building level.10 Therefore, the tools related to 
the sustainable renovation decision-making were categorized into two groups: 
1. Sustainable renovation decision support tools 
2. Tools for sustainability and performance assessment

==PAGE:12==
3 
 
The tools and their properties are first summarized in a table, followed by a short description of the 
tools’ principles. Different stages in the renovation process require different assessment tools.11 The 
second group of tools is typically used later in the process. However, the tools can serve for setting 
up the project goals to be reached and therefore can be involved in the design phase/decision-making. 
In order to track the decision support density in various stages, the ‘phase of utilization’ is one of the 
parameters of the first group due to a statement that “Most tools available tend to be most useful in 
a later stage of the planning process, whereas tools for the early design phases are rare,” by 
Bergman I. and Oy M. (2011).11 
Other parameters are ‘amount of criteria’ to record the complexity of the tools and ‘level of difficulty 
to read the results’ as it is believed that the users need to have easily given data.12 
The preservation has still the top priority in the renovation projects. Two methods serving for 
evaluation of the architectural and historical values are described as an example. Survey of 
Architectural Values in the Environment (SAVE)13 method was introduced in 1997 and served for 
evaluation of the architectural values of Danish buildings and assessment of the level of preservation. 
PETRA14 method can serve for evaluation of the quality of the historic building conservation together 
with the economic impact on tenants. It can also be used for the comparison of different intervention 
scenarios based on economic criteria. 
 Sustainable renovation decision support tools 
This group is typically performed through more or less advanced Microsoft Excel sheets. This 
category serves for an understanding of different scenarios’ presentation and as an inspiration for the 
new decision support tool proposal. Therefore, the examples of scenarios’ analyses are presented. 
Several decision support tools were created before 2005 as a result of former EU directive on the 
energy performance of buildings.15 These tools, namely EPIQR+16,17, INVESTIMMO18, TOBUS19,20 
and XENIOS15, serve for evaluation of the building deterioration followed by a proposal for suitable 
refurbishment and retrofitting actions for each building element. These tools are powerful decision-
making support tools suitable for survey and early design phases. However, they are lacking the 
sustainable holistic and life-cycle approach and are not developed further.

2.1.1 European Retrofit Advisor21,22 
This online tool allows quick evaluation of improvement scenarios for apartment buildings from all 
three aspects of sustainability. Several predefined values can be set and 3 scenarios, namely Repair, 
Retrofit and Reconstruction can be compared. Total evaluation describes the sustainability of the 
renovation action through the remaining service life with the 3 pillars (it is possible to set various 
priorities to different pillars) and resulting energy labels for the scenarios.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 2 Example of final comparison of three scenarios – European Retrofit Advisor21 
2.1.2 IRP - The Integrated Renovation Process23,24 
This methodology consists of 3 phases:  
1. Study of homeowners’ values and house profiling (tool suggests 9 semi-open questions) 
2. Generation and evaluation of renovation strategies (using tool BEopt31) based on multi-
criteria analysis and evaluation of a building from expert and homeowners’ perspectives.  
3. Synthesis and selection of the best strategy. The results are presented by a set of scale graphs 
using HERMIONE14 methodology - a qualitative and constructivist multiple criteria analysis 
method. Not clearly comparable scenarios have to stay out of the presentation. The 
environmental pillar is not considered in this method. 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3 Example of comparison analyses (to the left) and final presentation after the synthesis (to the right), A-
Excellent, G-Very Poor using The Integrated Renovation Process23

2.1.3 REHABILI-Tool25 
In this method, the benefits and co-benefits of various renovation scenarios are - based on building 
sustainability certification systems (2.2.1 BREEAM, 2.2.4 LEED) - organized as evaluation criteria, 
identified and ranked. Covering all three sustainability pillars, each criterion is assigned a fixed 
relative weight. The criteria for a specific pillar are brought together and recalculated, resulting in 
value from 0 to 1. It is also possible to gain this value for global analysis as can be seen in Figure 4. 
 
Figure 4 Example of global scenarios’ analysis with REHABILI-Tool25 
2.1.4 Renobuild27,28   
This methodology considers all three pillars while evaluating different renovation alternatives. Tools 
for ENV and SOC analyses were developed within the project, ECO is evaluated by an already 
existing Swedish tool for LCC. The three pillars are first evaluated separately and afterwards brought 
together. The tools used for the three pillars’ assessment can be exchanged. Renobuild is inspired by 
the methodology used in MultiOpt tool26 – Pareto optimization32. This method brings the ECO (LCC) 
and ENV (LCA) results into one graph together with the same wage. The social pillar is shown in 
the size of bubbles. 
 
Figure 5 Example of alternatives’ comparison inspired by Pareto optimization methodology (Social aspects are 
indicated by the size of bubbles, smaller means worse from a social perspective) - Renobuild28 
Global Analysis
==PAGE:15==

2.1.5 RENO-EVALUE29,30 
This value-based tool does not include any technical calculations and unlike the tools presented 
above, it does not compare different renovation alternatives. The tool can be used for example for 
matching expectations between different stakeholders and defining success criteria for a project. This 
communication tool covers all three sustainability pillars. The factors are divided into 8 parameters, 
which can be rated by different stakeholders from 1 to 5 and presented in a spider web as can be seen 
in Figure 6. 
 
Figure 6 Example of evaluation of one alternative by different stakeholders – RENO-EVALUE29 
 
 Tools for sustainability and performance assessment  
Building assessment tools and certification systems are sometimes discussed in terms of 
generations.33 The first generation was focusing on topics supporting the planning design, 
construction and green buildings’ marketing (such as energy efficiency, environmental or health 
issues). Several studies were conducted on this group, revealing environmental tools such as Athena, 
BEAT or BEES.34 The second generation (also referred to as top-down approach) follows the three 
dimensions of sustainability32,33 described at the beginning of this chapter. 
Between 2010 and 2012, a research project SuPerBuildings was conducted concerning sustainability 
assessment and benchmarking methods. The project investigated current tools and society 
understanding and proposed solutions how to improve the actual situation. More than 60 assessment 
methodologies were investigated leading to the identification of more than 500 indicators used 
worldwide.35,36,37

The following table provides the overview of several building sustainability certification systems. 
These tools are typically more advanced than the tools presented in the first group and contain many 
criteria and weighing system – often resulting in a single final value. The criteria used in presented 
methods were investigated and are listed in Appendix A. These tools enable sustainability 
comparison of different buildings among each other and thus create a drive in the building market 
through increasing the buildings’ competitiveness.38

2.2.1 BREEAM39 
Building Research Establishment Environmental Assessment Method is the world’s longest 
established method for rating and certification of buildings’ sustainability with a major focus on the 
environmental pillar. It also allows certification of buildings in different stages and different 
categories resulting into label outstanding, excellent, very good, good, pass, and unclassified. The 
score is in credits, transformed to final percentages. 
2.2.2 CASBEE40 
Comprehensive Assessment System for Built Environment Efficiency evaluates the environmental 
performance and results into one of five grades: Superior (S), Very Good (A), Good (B+), Slightly 
Poor (B-) and Poor (C). It works with the methodology of environmental quality vs environmental 
load and does not assess the economic pillar. Within the CASBEE family evaluation, the whole 
building Life Cycle (LC) is considered. 
2.2.3 DGNB41,42 
Deutsche Gesellschaft für nachhaltiges Bauen (German sustainable building council) is a 
sustainability certification system considering 61 criteria in 6 groups, which are weighed into final 
percentage and lead to certification of the project as DGNB gold, silver or bronze. DGNB system as 
the only one gives as much importance to the economic aspect in the evaluation as it does to the 
ecological criteria. It also works with the whole life cycle. 
==PAGE:17==
8 
 
2.2.4 LEED43  
Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design is another benchmarking system, which allows 
comparison of building projects within the same type of project and phase. In the Building Design 
and Construction category, 57 parameters are divided into 8 groups, contributing to the final amount 
of points. The project is certified according to the reached amount of points as platinum, gold, silver 
or certified according to the points from the environmental and social point of view. 
2.2.5 LEnSE44 
European research project LEnSE introduced a methodology for Label for Environmental, Social 
and Economic Buildings. The final certification can be done through a single letter. The alphabet is 
also used to evaluate the three pillars or 11 categories one by one (consisting of 56 parameters). 
2.2.6 OPEN HOUSE36,45-48 
Based on an analysis and critical evaluation of existing assessment methods of building sustainability 
standards and activities, the OPEN HOUSE methodology was created. 46 parameters are considered 
based on a list of 560 indicators, using the same groups as DGNB. An online platform serves as an 
assessment tool with maximum score 100 points after parameters’ weighing and certification as 
‘Assessed with OPENHOUSE.’ 
2.2.7 SBTool49,50 
This generic building assessment framework serves for rating the sustainable performance of 
building projects and sites. For the building projects, 7 main issue areas are divided into several 
parameters. The amount of parameters can differ according to the project phase. The issues can be 
presented in a form of a radar or a pie diagram or a relative performance result is presented in the 
form of a letter. It contains two distinct assessment modules – one for the site assessment and another 
one for the building assessment.  
 
 
==PAGE:18==
9 
 
3 OBJECTIVES AND METHODOLOGY 
Based on the literature review, it was concluded, that several sustainable decision support tools are 
available but considerations about building demolition and replacement with a new building are very 
limited. 
This fact is the starting point for the methodology in this research. The main idea is to include the 
demolition of an existing building and its replacement with a new one as one of the alternatives in 
the renovation design process. The methodology should not be used without former assessment of 
heritage and cultural values and is not valid for a high level of preservation of an assessed building.  
It is believed that satisfactory amount of tools is already available on the market11 and development 
of a new tool (as it was originally intended) would not be beneficial. Therefore, it is rather 
investigated how to implement the new scenario as another alternative into one of the already existing 
methods.  
An important aspect of the methodology is that the sustainability assessment tool serving for 
evaluation of solution alternatives should be used by experts and therefore the input may be 
complicated. However, the simplicity of results’ presentation is extremely important to ensure the 
understanding of the decision-making stakeholders with limited knowledge of the building sector.33   
As the goal is to evaluate whether it is more sustainable to renovate an existing building or to tear it 
down and replace it with a new building, a renovation scenario must be found first in order to 
compare the two alternatives. This can be done through the tools described in chapter 2.1. The deep 
renovation means large energy savings and is often the best renovation scenario. In a new imaginary 
scenario, it is considered that the existing building is torn down and replaced with a new one. This 
new building meets the performance requirements of nowadays standards, while keeping as many 
parameters, as they would be in the renovation scenario, as possible, in order to ensure the 
comparability of the cases. The performance requirements of a building are mostly related to the 
building structure, safety, energy performance, technical services and indoor environment quality. 
In the chapters 4 and 5, the methodology is applied to a real case and discussed further. The only 
input for the case study is a Building Information Modelling (BIM) model in REVIT (computer 
program for BIM) of the building and all the necessary calculations are done as part of the analysis 
– which is how it would be done in a design process. 
As a part of this research, the second imaginary scenario was investigated for the case study in order 
to examine the processes’ differences. In this scenario, the existing building is also demolished and 
replaced with a new building. However, the parameters of the new building are kept the same as in 
the renovation scenario. It should be noticed that these two new scenarios are only imaginary. The 
factual replacement approach is discussed further in chapter 6.  
Very important aspect in the sustainable development is the assessment from the whole life cycle’s 
point of view – covering all stages through which something passes during its lifetime.51 The three 
scenarios were investigated within 3 different time spans after the measures were taken5,25 – 30, 50 
and 100 years to examine the impact of different life cycle phases. 100-year time span is covering 
the whole life cycle of a mixed-use building52 and the end of this study period considers the 
demolition of the building. 50 years is based on the DGNB methodology. Having 9 scenarios in total, 
==PAGE:19==
10 
 
the first step in decision-making – determination of solution alternatives – is now completed. The 
next step is the criteria assessment on the building level. 
The sustainability evaluation tools contain many parameters in order to ensure comparability among 
various buildings. Because the proposed methodology only compares small amount of scenarios, not 
so many parameters are investigated. The initial list of evaluation parameters to investigate is the 
result of literature review33,35 and analysis of sustainability evaluation parameters listed in Appendix 
A and is presented in Table 4.  
Table 4 Initial list of the evaluation parameters35

The parameters in italic are considered to be the same or very similar to each other in all three 
scenarios. Inspired by the method used in Life Cycle Cost Analysis (LCCA)7 , these parameters stay 
out of the comparison.  
The social pillar is often described as the most difficult one to quantify.53 Comparing the three 
scenarios, many parameters in the social pillar will be the same. The indoor environment quality was 
investigated through IDA Indoor Climate and Energy (IDA ICE) simulation tool. This tool was also 
used for the energy performance simulation, which is a necessary input for a building assessment 
through the building’s LC. 
==PAGE:20==
11 
 
The rest of the parameters can be divided into environmental and economic issues. Method Life 
Cycle Assessment (LCA) and Life Cycle Cost (LCC) can be used for analysis of the environmental 
and economic pillars respectively (as it is done in tool Renobuild described in 2.1.4). These two 
methods were explored within the special course called ‘Life Cycle Management of Buildings’ and 
are explained further in Appendix Z. The methods are shortly described in this chapter followed by 
their general considerations in the research methodology. Several assumptions in the analysis are 
case-specific and are described later in chapter 4. 
In 2015, a sustainable package for the building political strategy was released in Denmark. This 
package contains a general guidance on sustainable construction, two guides on LCA and LCC and 
basic report on the building’s LC. Two tools for LCA and LCCA, namely LCAbyg and LCCbyg, 
were developed as part of this research as well.54  These tools were used in the analysis. 
 Life Cycle Assessment 
Life Cycle Assessment, also referred to as Life Cycle Analysis, is a complex tool that assists building 
engineers with evaluation of Environmental Impact (EI) of a building through all the life cycle 
phases. Several LCA methodologies are available. These methods usually follow ISO 14040,55 
family of LCA standards.56 In the building construction industry mainly process-based LCA methods 
are used. One of these methods is Cradle-to-Grave, which can also be called Full LCA, considering 
life-cycle stages from resource extraction to use and disposal phase.8 This method is used in this 
research. 
According to EN ISO 14040, LCA consists of 4 steps: Goal and scope definition, inventory analysis, 
impact assessment and interpretation. 
3.1.1 Goal and Scope Definition 
Within the first step, a functional unit is defined in order to ensure the comparability of LCA results 
for different alternatives. 3 different functional units are investigated in the case study as the building 
with the same room areas and functions serving for 30, 50 and 100 years respectively. The three 
scenarios described earlier in chapter 3 are then 3 different strategies on how to provide this service 
to the users. 
Many LCA studies in the building industry follow the DGNB International simplified methodology 
(which is part of the certification system described in 2.2.3). System boundaries and life cycle phases 
in the study are considered according to the following table.

3.1.2 Inventory Analysis, Impact Assessment and Interpretation 
The inventory analysis is the core of LCA with regards to the work process. In this phase, data are 
collected in order to quantify relevant inputs (regarding, for example, raw material or energy) and 
outputs (discharges to water and soil, emissions to air and others) of a product system. The inventory 
analysis requires software tools with a large focus on databases.  
Tool LCAbyg developed by the Danish government for environmental assessment of buildings was 
used initially. The tool uses a limited database based on Ökobau (German database for construction 
materials and building services58) with several preset building elements. It is also possible to insert 
inventory manually into the tool. Due to certain limitations of the tool, a tool Quantis Suite 2.2 (QS 
2.2) was recommended by Morten Birkved, the associate professor at DTU MAN Quantitative 
Sustainability Assessment. 
This tool uses the most famous LCA database – ecoinvent59 and follows IMPACT 2002+ 
methodology evaluating the impact assessment in terms of damage categories as can be seen in 
Figure 7. Social LCA (SLCA), which is used for the analysis, partially covers the social pillar.53 
==PAGE:22==
13 
 
 
Figure 7 Overall scheme of the IMPACT 2002+ vQ2.2 framework linking LCA results via the midpoint categories 
with damage categories, based on Jolliet et al.60,61 
x 
Human Health [DALY] The “human health” damage category is the sum of 5 top midpoint 
categories as can be seen in Figure 3 and is expressed in DALYs. “Disability-Adjusted Life 
Years” characterizes the disease severity, accounting for both mortality and morbidity. The 
human health average damage is 0,0071 DALY/point.60  
x 
Ecosystem quality [PDF.m2.y] “PDF·m2·y” (“Potentially Disappeared Fraction of species 
over a certain amount of m2 during a certain amount of year”) is the unit to measure the 
impacts on ecosystems. The PDF·m2·y represents the 3 years of life lost distributed over the 
overall population and NOT per person.60 
x 
Climate change [kg CO2-eq] change can also be presented as global warming with the units 
“kg equivalent of a reference substance s.” It expresses the amount of a reference substance 
s that equals the impact of the considered pollutant within the midpoint category studies (e.g., 
the 9 Global Warming Potential on a 100-y scale of fossil-based methane is 27,75 times 
higher than CO2, thus its CF is 27,75 kg CO2-eq).60 
x 
Resources [MJ] Resulting from “non-renewable energy” and “mineral extraction”, this 
category measures the amount of energy needed to extract the resource or for another 
purpose.60 
Damage categories can be finally interpreted separately in the form of graphs or tables or combined 
and  result in a single value. During the combination process, the weighing system can be applied 
which might lead to miscalculation of some values. Many professionals believe that it is better to 
report the impacts in each of the categories.8  
 
 
==PAGE:23==
14 
 
3.1.3 LCA as Part of the Methodology 
The process of the analysis used in the methodology is described in Figure 8. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Software QS 2.2 offers large flexibility in the inputs supported by the Ecoinvent 2.2 inventory 
database. The inventory in the analysis is inspired by equivalent items from the inventory database. 
Compared to LCAbyg, which includes life spans of the materials and automatically replaces the 
materials at the end of their life time, the life span is put manually in this program. The materials 
with shorter life span than study period are replaced at the end of their life span and steps 2a, 3 and 
4 are repeated for the specific materials. If the life span equals to the study period, the end of life of 
the material is considered. 
BIM model in REVIT offers automatic materials take off in m3. Ecoinvent 2.2 database works mostly 
with materials in kg and therefore all the data from the BIM model had to be recalculated.62 
Transport in manufacturing phase is included in the Ecoinvent 2.2 inventory database. According to 
Morten Birkved “The energy related to manufacturing of building elements is usually an estimate 
and there is a lack of measurements in this section.” Therefore, this energy is not included in the case 
LCA. Transportation from a local storage to the site and from the site to an incineration plant was 
investigated within the special course and it showed minor influence in the total impact. The 
transportation is not included as it is proposed in the DGNB methodology (see Table 5).   
Resulting total annual energy consumption from IDA ICE simulations for different scenarios was 
used as an input through the whole study period63. User’s consumption was not considered according 
to Table 5 and lighting was assumed to be 3,3 kWh/m2a64. The forecasting of the development of 
energy production was simplified63 – the energy production is assumed to be the same through the 
whole study period. 
Recyclable materials representing the major part of building elements are assumed to be recycled 
because recycling of the building materials is essential in decreasing the environmental impact of 
buildings.1 The rest of the materials goes to municipal incineration plant directly and landfilling is 
assumed the last option.

 Life Cycle Cost  
LCC presents all significant costs of ownership of an item, system or a facility over its economic life 
expressed in term of equivalent currency.7 Many investors make nowadays decisions based on initial 
cost, which is only a portion (often small) of the total cost over an asset’s LC.65 LCCA is especially 
useful for comparison of different project alternatives over a certain study period or the whole life 
cycle.66 The profitability of the solutions depends on the time and can change the final proposal after 
analyzing the whole calculation period.   
There are several economic evaluation methods. The lowest LCC, which is the most straightforward 
and easy to interpret one,66 was investigated in this research. An important term in this method is Net 
Present Value (NPV), which shows recalculation of the cost estimates over calculation period to a 
single time, usually the start point. The process called ‘discounting the future costs to a present value’ 
is used because the current value of money paid in future is usually lower than the value of money 
paid today.52 The discounting of money paid in future is influenced by Discount Rate (DR), which is 
an expression of expected inflation, interest and future risk. Further explanation can be found in 
Appendix Z. 
3.2.1 LCC Classification 
In order to compare different alternatives, consistent classification is necessary. EN 15686-6 
terminology was perceived as inconsistent and the cost classification is far from practical experiences 
in Nordic countries.67 As the Danish tool for LCCA called LCCbyg was used, the classification used 
in this software is considered. 
As a result of the joint Nordic project, the main LCC categories were divided into 8 categories: 
Capital, Administration, Operation, Maintenance, Development, Consumption, Cleaning and 
Service.67 The classification is based on the Swedish SfB classification.68 The experience points out 
that a general account plan should be universal so it can be adapted to individual building owners.54 
In the template for project design, the costs are divided into: 
x 
Consulting, programming and builder 
x 
Building elements 
x 
Fixtures and equipment 
x 
Management 
x 
Supplies 
x 
Cleaning 
From the LCC point of view, the life cycle can be divided into acquisition, use and maintenance, 
renewal and adaptation and disposal phases.69 
 
 
==PAGE:25==

Capital Cost 
The capital cost is determined as the building construction (including decommissioning by the end 
of use of the facilities), the indirect cost for the owner and indirect cost for the contractor. 70 
For the building construction, a cost was estimated using Sigma Cost Estimator71 with V&S Price 
books72 (commonly used for cost estimates in Denmark) as libraries. The renovation scenario uses 
price book ‘Bygningsren. Husbygning 2015 brutto,’ the two new scenarios use price book 
‘Nybyggeri – Husbygning Brutto 2016.’ These books are used for cost estimates on a building level.

Figure 9 Process of LCCA conducted within the research 
==PAGE:26==
17 
 
The price books73,74 use m2 as units in most of the cases so the materials take-off from the BIM model 
had to be converted. The final cost for a building element is a result of quantity take off, labor hours, 
labor rates and materials prices applying price script according to the amount of material (larger 
amount of material typically means lower cost). Data for equivalent elements or typical generic data 
are adopted in the V&S Price book library. In some cases, a replacement is considered as demolition 
of existing elements and application of new ones. Thin layers, foils, screws and other connection 
elements are not included in the cost estimation.70 Cost of technical services was estimated based on 
value/m2 for different systems and functions in V&S Price books.  
The indirect costs were determined as percentages of the building elements. The indirect cost for the 
owner (design fees, permits, land acquisition, legal fees, administration costs etc.70) was set to 15%. 
This value was taken from experience by the company cooperating at the master thesis, presenting 
the design fees. The rest of the indirect costs for the owner was considered the same for the scenarios. 
The indirect cost related to the building construction is difficult to estimate. In the case of renovation, 
this cost often increases during the construction because of problems related to the existing structure 
not discovered earlier during the survey. In the case of replacement the demolition of the building 
brings extra cost, on the other hand, the construction of the new building can be faster thanks to the 
equipment used at the building site, which could not be utilized in case of renovation. The costs 
related to the duration of the construction would require deeper analysis. Finally, the indirect cost for 
the contractor was set to 10% in all three cases.73,74   
In preliminary estimates, the design contingencies are typically included in forms of percentages 
too.75 These are set to 10% (of building construction + indirect cost for the owner + indirect cost for 
the contractor) in the case of the new scenarios and increased to 15% in case of the renovation. From 
experience, the renovation shows larger risk for an increase in the initial cost during the construction 
due to problems in existing construction. 
Operation, Maintenance and Renewal Cost 
Maintenance of building elements is annually calculated in LCCbyg as a percentage of the initial 
cost of the building element.76 It is discounted according to Eq.1 and Eq.2 in chapter 4.2.4 in 
Appendix Z and all the years are summed together for the whole study period.  
Replacement of building elements occurs automatically and is discounted according to Eq,1.in 
Appendix Z. There is a small difference from the LCA calculation. If an element has life span 30 
years, the replacement would be counted in only if the study period is >30.  
Consumption 
The energy consumption is calculated as the total energy consumption in the whole study period 
based on IDA ICE results for annual energy consumption (excluding user’s consumption and 
assuming electricity consumption 3,3 kWh/m2a for lighting64). Prices for energy supply are used as 
predefined in the LCCbyg template.52 
Disposal cost 
In the special course related to the master thesis, it was found out that the NPV of the demolition cost 
considered in 100 years is discounted to an insignificant value (in the renovation case < 0,07% of 
total LCC) and therefore is neglected in the analysis. 
==PAGE:27==
18 
 
Price Development and Discount Rate 
LCCbyg template suggests price development for different cost groups according to Danish 
Statistics, taking the inflation of costs into account.54 The suggested values for price development 
are used in the study.  
Sensitivity analysis of DR was conducted in the special course as the DR has a large influence in 
LCCA.52 The results show that the lower the DR is, the higher the influence of the results is with 
regards to the calculation period. Recommended stable value of 5,5% is used for the nominal DR in 
the analysis assuming general price development at 2%.52  
Salvage value 
Salvage value (also referred to as disposal value or residual value) of an asset is an estimated amount 
of the property expected to be received at the end of its useful life.77 It is often estimated as zero.78 
In order to estimate the value of property in future, a depreciation method is used – cost distribution 
of tangible assets over the life of assets.79 Straight-line depreciation is the default method used for 
this purposes based on the following formula.

ܷݏ݂݁ݑ݈ ܮ݂݅݁

4 CASE STUDY 
The methodology is now applied to a case study. General introduction, schematic floor plans and 
sections are described in section 4.1 as the Scenario 1 represents the real and reference case. 
Scenarios 2 and 3 are following the general description and differences from the Scenario 1 are 
described for each case later in the chapter. At the end of chapter 4, all the cases are summed up. 
 Scenario 1 – Reference Case 
The object of the investigation is Care Center Farum Midtpunkt. The care center is located in blocks 
12 and 13 in Farum Midtpunkt – an area of approximately 35 ha consisting of 27 blocks in total from 
the 1970s. These two terraced blocks went through a deep renovation in 2014 as a result of 
cooperation between the municipality Farum and the housing association managing the housing area. 
The municipality was looking for some space for a new care center and the housing association 
needed some middle-sized apartments which had not been part of the original design and became 
more demanded nowadays. 
This study only covers the block 12, which represents the typical block of the area. The building of 
total gross floor area of 7676 m2 contains 87 apartments of different sizes and part of the care center 
(originally having 57 apartments only, 22 of which were maisonettes). Every apartment has access 
to a terrace (on western façade) or a balcony (to the east). The rest of the care center is positioned in 
the adjacent block 13, which is connected to the block 12 via a bridge. Block 12 consists of 4 floors 
with parking on the ground floor, housing in the top floor and care center in the rest of the building. 
The building is 140 m long and 31 m wide, longer façades face east and west. 
BIM model and scanned original drawings together with some necessary information were provided 
by NIRAS – the consultant engineers for the renovation. These documents served as the input for the 
investigation. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 12 Care Center Farum Midtpunkt81 
==PAGE:29==
20 
 
The load-bearing construction is made of concrete. The hollow core slabs are carried by concrete 
beams and columns on the ground floor and transverse walls in the rest of the floors. The building 
was originally insulated and the prefabricated elements were used, as it was usual at the time of the 
building erection. The ground floor and the first floor were connected by three external round 
staircases made of concrete. There were 11 concrete staircases between the first and second floor. 
The third floor was offering the top floor of 22 maisonettes and could be accessed through smaller 
staircases within the apartments.  
The original staircases were torn down and 4 new ones were built up, connecting all 4 floors because 
of the fire safety requirements. 2 elevators were installed as well. The building was previously 
renovated due to hazardous materials (polychlorinated biphenyl), so no dangerous instances were 
discovered in the building during the current pre-retrofit survey. Some of the skylights were removed. 
In the third floor, they were replaced by a non-transparent construction and additional windows were 
installed on the eastern facade to lighten the corridor. Two wings in the 1st floor were removed to 
enlarge the terraces. All non-load bearing internal walls were demolished and the layout was changed 
as can be seen on following figures and in Appendix B. New steel beams were introduced to ensure 
the stability of the construction due to changes in the interior layout. 
The building after renovation contains parking and technical services on the ground floor. The care 
center with offices, shared areas and in total 70 small residential units of less than 40 m2 in the first 
and second floor. In the third floor, there are shared areas, 4 apartments for couples and 13 dwellings 
for one person.

Air Handling Units (AHUs) located on the ground floor. The building is today heated by radiators 
same as before the renovation. 
Based on Building Regulations (BR) 201082 and BR 20154, the building was treated in the analysis 
as a multi-use building. The first and the second floor serve the purpose of the care center. The third 
floor is considered to be a residential floor.

4.1.1 Energy Performance – Scenario 1 and 2 
The energy performance received special attention as it is to have the largest influence in LCA and 
LCC.7  
IDA ICE is a powerful simulation tool providing the energy performance and indoor climate 
simulation. It is a dynamic multi-zone simulation application – meaning that it allows the simulation 
of the entire building.83 The tool can handle a large amount of data. The larger and more detailed the 
building model is, the longer the simulation takes. For this reason, several simplifications and 
methods were followed. 
Thin layers (for example vapor barrier) were not included in the simulation since these layers have 
an insignificant impact on the energy performance. However, the layers with high vapor resistance 
and their positioning in the construction are extremely important from the moisture transfer point of 
view. All the constructions were therefore evaluated with Glaser’s method84 and it was checked that 
there is no risk of condensation and the potential creation of mold in the building. 
External and unheated areas (exterior parking, terraces and balconies) were not considered in the 
simulation as these areas do not present any influence on the heat transfer. A simplification in the 
building model was examined. Starting with the apartment as the smallest zone, it turned out that the 
smallest module for the simulation is the module presented in Figure 18-Figure 21 without significant 
influence on the result.  
The multi-use building was divided into two types of zones –apartments with bathrooms and shared 
areas. The indoor climate parameters (the temperature set points, air exchange rates aso.) were 
following recommendations in EN 15251.85 All input parameters used for the simulation can be 
found in Appendix C.1. 
Resulting annual primary energy consumption for heating, ventilation, cooling and domestic hot 
water for the care center is 56,9 kWh/m2 and for the top floor it is 51,7 kWh/m2. The building is 
meeting the energy requirements for new buildings in BR 2010, using primary energy factors relevant 
for that year.  
 Scenario 2 
Scenario 2 represents the demolition of an existing building and its replacement with a new building 
having the same parameters as Scenario 1. Therefore, it follows the parameters of Scenario 1. The 
detailed input for the energy performance and the indoor climate simulation, the LCA and the LCCA 
can be found in Appendix C.1, Appendix D.2 and Appendix E.2 respectively. 
 Scenario 3 
Scenario 3 also represents a new building replacing the existing building after its demolition. This 
building follows the requirements for a current new building in BR 2015 while staying as similar to 
the Scenario 1, as possible. 
The real case uses a construction principle still used nowadays, so the principal is used in Scenario 
3 too. Steel beams installed to ensure the stability of the existing building in Scenario 1 were 
considered in this scenario too, to ensure the comparability of the cases. The floor height is increased 
to 2,5 m which is a required value for the new buildings. The zones are heated by floor heating, 
==PAGE:39==
30 
 
which is a typical element for space heating in new buildings in Denmark. Floor heating has better 
potentials in connecting of an object to low-temperature district heating which is being introduced in 
Denmark as a sustainable district heating distribution.86,87 Apart from the heating system, the 
technical services are the same as in Scenario 1 and 2. They stay out of the LCA based on simplified 
DGNB methodology57. Within LCC, the demolition of the existing technical services is excluded as 
it would be the same for all three scenarios. Construction of new technical services is included in 
LCC. 
4.3.1 Energy Performance – Scenario 3 
Other changes in the building properties are a result of an optimization of the indoor climate with the 
energy performance – which is a usual practice in the design of new buildings nowadays.88 The 
optimization in this case study looks into what indoor climate quality can be achieved by certain 
technical services and the thermal properties of building envelope, while meeting the requirements 
for energy performance and keeping as many parameters as they are in the real case, as possible.   
The area of the building envelope in the real case contains a large amount of glazing. Windows are 
generally considered a sensitive area of the building envelope and require big attention in the design 
process.89 Unlike the rest of the constructions, they also bring daylight to the room and provide the 
necessary visual connection of the occupants with the exterior. Apart from the Light Transmittance 
(LT) important for the daylight factor, the solar energy transmittance (g-value) and the thermal 
conductivity (U-value) of the glazing are important from the heat transfer point of view. Better 
glazing properties (higher LT, lower U-value and g-value) bring an increase in the initial cost. In 
order to keep the original appearance with large window openings, windows with very good 
properties and window shadings in summer were assumed.  
The thermal properties of the constructions are presented in Table 7 followed by drawings of the 
building module of this case. The detailed input for the energy performance and the indoor climate 
simulation, the LCA and the LCCA can be found in Appendix C.3, Appendix D.3 and Appendix E.3, 
respectively. 
Table 7 U-values of constructions – Scenario 3

The air exchange was decreased compared to the recommended values as the result of the 
optimization. While providing the good indoor environment, the final annual primary energy 
consumption for heating, venting, cooling and domestic hot water is 40,1 kWh/m2 for the care center 
and 29,7 kWh/m2 for the dwellings in the top floor. The building meets the energy requirement for 
low energy buildings 2015 according to BR 2010. In BR 2015 this is a requirement for new buildings. 
The primary energy factors for 2010 were used in order to ensure the comparability of the scenarios.

5 RESULTS 
 Case study analysis 
The 3 scenarios (Sc 1, Sc 2 and Sc 3) were investigated within 30, 50 and 100 years time spans (3 
functional units as defined in 3.2.2). The results for the Indoor Environment Quality (IEQ), the LCA 
and the LCC of the different cases are presented now. 
5.1.1 Indoor Environment Quality 
In EN 15251, buildings are divided into 4 classes according to provided IEQ. Typical classes for the 
case study would be II and III - class II, applicable in new buildings and renovation, providing the 
normal level of expectation and class III an acceptable, moderate level of expectation in existing 
buildings. The evaluation criteria are thermal comfort (Indoor Temperature (Ti) within a design 
range), CO2 concentration, humidity, lighting, daylight and acoustics.  
It was concluded that the most critical zone regarding the IEQ would be an apartment in the top floor 
facing to the west with many window openings. The required values for the criteria in dwellings, 
which were investigated in the case study, are listed in Table 10. The resulting hourly values across 
the whole year are then evaluated in the form of % of occupied hours when the criteria are outside 
the specific range. In order to reach a concrete IEQ category, the criteria can vary from the required 
range within a specific deviation (recommended value 5%).85 
Table 10 Recommended design criteria85 
Requirements for dwellings 
Class II 
Class III 
Minimum temperature Ti for heating season 
20°C 
18°C 
Maximum temperature Ti for non-heating season 
26°C 
27°C 
CO2 concentration [ppm above outdoor] 
500 
800 
Design Relative Humidity (RH) [%] 
25-60 
20-70 
Daylight factor 
>3% 
>3% 
Deviation 
5% 
5% 
 
This analysis only evaluates the current situation and does not look into the life cycle. With a proper 
operation and maintenance, this IEQ could be ideally provided within the lifetime. Often, the IEQ is 
decreased through the use phase by building deterioration, inadequate maintenance and users’ 
behavior being different than assumed in the analysis.91  
Figure 27 shows the results of the IDA ICE simulations and the daylight assessment in Velux 
visualizer92. Sc 1 and Sc 2 show the same results. There is no deviation with regards to the 
requirements for CO2 concentration and RH because the ventilation system in the simulation used 
the controllers to ensure this. Looking at the thermal comfort, Sc 1 and Sc 2 bring too much 
overheating in the summer and do not meet the required deviation of 5% because of a lot of solar 
gain through the windows and heat income through the building envelope (compared to Sc 3). Sc 3 
meets the requirements for the Class II. In all the scenarios, enough daylight is brought to the room. 
In Sc 3, the amount of daylight is due to the thickness of exterior walls.

environmental impact in all damage categories except ‘human health’ (Sc 1 30 and Sc 3 30). In this

It can be seen that the demolition at the starting point has a small influence (due to the assumption 
that many materials are recycled and therefore do not have a large impact), compared to the energy 
consumption which causes the major contribution. This fact causes the previously described 
phenomenon that Sc 3 has overall smaller environmental impact then Sc 1. The difference between 
Sc 1 and Sc 2 in Figure 28 documents the importance of the embodied energy in the existing 
construction in the renovation case (more precisely the material savings in the renovation case as 
explained later).   
Looking at the changes within the whole LC, it can be seen that the contribution of the energy 
consumption increases compared to the impact caused by the materials. This creates the growing 
difference between the environmental impacts of Sc 1/Sc 2 and Sc 3 through the life cycle as can be 
seen in Figure 28. The smaller the energy consumption of a building is, the larger the percentage 
impact caused by the building materials is. The energy consumption contributes more to the 
’resources’ and ‘climate change’, while the materials contribute more to the ‘human health’ and 
‘ecosystem quality’ categories. The energy consumption impact varies between 34% (HH3 in Figure 
29) to 84% (CC1 in Figure 31) and the materials contribution from 66% to 16% in the same cases.  
The absolute impacts of ‘End of life cycle-materials’ and ‘Manufacturing phase’ within one scenario 
stay the same within 30 and 50 years study periods. This means that all the differences in the total 
impacts of these study periods are caused by the use phase. In the 100-year study period, the impact 
caused by the demolition also comes into play projected in ‘End of life cycle-materials’ phase. 
Looking at the reference case where 100 years represents 100%, this impact related to the material 
life cycle (combination of the exchanged material during the use phase and the demolished material 
in 100 years) is maximum 6% in different study periods (around 5% deviation from 30 and 50% in 
the shorter periods.)  
Overview of the data obtained from QS 2.2 is presented in a table in Appendix F. It can be seen, that 
the impact of exchanged materials during the use phase grows significantly from the 30-year to the 
50-year study period. This is because many materials have the end of their life at 40 years. The 
demolition in 100 years from starting point has a smaller influence than the impact caused by the 
building operation with regards to the material (see Figure 30 and Figure 31). 
As the 50-year study period is recommended in the DGNB methodology42, this study period is 
analyzed further to compare the 3 scenarios among each other. 
Looking at the ‘end of life cycle-materials’ phase in Figure 30 not a big difference between the 
scenarios is noticeable. The difference in this phase is mainly caused by demolished concrete due to 
the assumption that a high percentage of materials is recycled and therefore brings a small impact. 
The material savings related to Sc 1 are more visible in the newly installed material (Manufacturing 
phase). 
In this phase, Sc 2 and Sc 3 show higher values due to the larger amount of materials. The materials 
with the highest influence are different for the damage categories due to the different properties and 
impacts of the materials. Some of the materials with the highest influence are concrete, steel, mineral 
wool and polystyrene as can be seen in Appendix F – ‘LCA – Materials contribution to 4 damage 
categories in manufacturing phase.’  
Regarding the ‘Use phase-Exchange,’ the most important materials belong to finishing and windows 
and doors. These materials were considered to have a smaller life cycle than the study period.  
==PAGE:51==
42 
 
The total energy consumption is divided into electricity, heating and lighting as can be seen in Figure 
32. For Sc 1 and Sc 2 the consumption is assumed to be the same. Heating shows the largest 
contribution to the total energy consumption. In Sc 3 the influence caused by heating is decreased 
due to the smaller heat loss and the overall properties of the building model. The ratio presented in 
Figure 32 stays the same across various study periods.

5.1.3 Life Cycle Cost 
Figure 33 shows the comparison of the 3 scenarios within the 3 study periods and the contribution of 
3 costs – Capital cost (decreased of discounted salvage value), Maintenance cost and Consumption 
cost. The reference scenario in 100 years (Sc 1 100) is set to 100% in the table below the graph. 
The combination of the large material mass and the larger energy consumption in Sc 2 causes that 
this solution has the highest LCC in all the study periods and Sc 3 shows the smallest value. It can 
be seen that in the total LCC, the energy consumption takes the major part from 50 up to 89%. 
Maintenance cost brings the lowest contribution out of the three presented costs varying from 2 to 
5%. The lower the energy consumption is, the higher the contribution of the building materials to the 
LCC is.  
The importance of the energy consumption grows with the length of a study period because the 
absolute capital cost stays fixed within one scenario and the maintenance shows a minor cost. The 
capital cost is the highest in Sc 3.

renovation case which results into lower price of some of the cost groups in the replacement case (it 
can be seen in Sc 1 and Sc 2 in graphs ‘Finishing’ and ‘Technical service’s in Appendix G.) 
5.1.4 Final Evaluation 
The Scenario 3 – demolition and replacement of the existing building with a new one, meeting the 
requirements of the current standards – shows overall the most sustainable solution from the three 
investigated scenarios. It provides a better quality of the indoor environment. In the environmental 
and the economic evaluation, the difference in the energy savings showed larger influence than the 
impact caused by the construction difference and results in the most efficient solution over all three 
study periods.  
It must be emphasized that the author acknowledges the limitations of the analysis, namely the 
assumptions and simplifications in LCA and LCC and limitations in the social pillar evaluation. The 
numbers presented in the inputs and outputs are rounded to maximum three decimals, but the full 
values were used in the calculations.  
 
 
==PAGE:56==
47 
 
 Proposal for the integration of a new element in 
sustainable decision-making 
This section is looking into how to integrate the previously described results and method into the 
decision-making process. The new imaginary scenarios could be counted as new alternatives 
compared in the tools described in 2.1. According to the DGNB methodology, which is widely used 
in sustainability assessments, the 50-year study period is considered in the analysis.57,93  
Tool Renobuild, described in 2.1.4, is used as an example so the three presented scenarios are 
compared as solution alternatives in this tool. In order to compare the scenarios according to the 
Renobuild method, it is necessary to present every pillar by one value only. The tool leaves some 
space for the way, how these values are obtained, which makes it very flexible. 
The economical aspect is covered by the total LCC for the alternatives. The environmental impact 
of the three scenarios was presented earlier in 4 damage categories. In the case of aggregation, a 
default weighting factor of one can be used to bring these categories together.60 The social pillar 
shown in Figure 38 compares the IEQ in the critical zone in the case study. Satisfaction of the 
occupants is shown as the size of the bubble (the larger the better from a social perspective). Taking 
the highest value in LCA and LCC and the lowest in IEQ as 100%, the comparison would according 
to Renobuild methodology look like this:  
 
Figure 38 Final comparison of 3 alternatives using Renobuild methodology (Social aspects are indicated by the 
size of bubbles, smaller means worse from a social perspective)28
