6 DISCUSSION AND FUTURE WORK 
State of the art 
The initial research pointed out that many tools for sustainable decision-making support during the 
building design were developed. Many of the tools are old and not developed further. There are 
several challenges in the implementation of these tools and sustainability into the building renovation 
design process. 
Renovation of a building is mostly a reaction to a technical problem in the building (for example 
elimination of moisture, expired service-life of components) or to complaints of users or tenants53 
(improving building aesthetics, improvement of air quality or high energy use27). It is often up to the 
owners, whether sustainability becomes part of a renovation project or not.53 Nowadays, the investors 
make their decisions based on the initial cost and the energy savings when discussing the solutions 
to the problems, evoking the renovation, without the holistic sustainable approach.94 The sustainable 
decision support tools are therefore extremely important during the discussion among various 
stakeholders to make the renovation more sustainable which is becoming more and more utilized.95 
The integration of different stakeholders is also judged as insufficient or initiated too late in the 
process33 and often brings a conflict between multiple goals14. Another issue in the design process is 
that the manufacturers, who develop the new ideas and better information to assure optimum 
decision, are not part of the discussion. 
More importantly, there is no common understanding of the concept of sustainability.47 Even the so-
called sustainability evaluation tools described in chapter 2.2 do not include all three pillars. 
Additionally, there is a vast amount of decision support tools available and used today, which focus 
on single aspects of sustainability or do not have an integrated, balanced approach to the 
sustainability evaluation.33,16 The amount of finally discovered sustainability tools is excluded of 
those which cannot be utilized for renovation projects, resulting in a very limited amount of tools 
which can be used for the sustainable decision-making support during the renovation process.53 These 
tools are not as famous as the building sustainability certification systems and their implementation 
in the design process is, therefore, complicated.33 One more issue related to the certification systems 
is that they were mostly developed for evaluation purposes and not as decision support tools. As they 
are often used for setting up the design targets, they strongly influence the design process and 
consequently the building industry.38  
Those tools, which consider all three pillars often require very complicated input and lack the 
transparency in presented results. As shown in Chapter 2, the sustainable renovation decision-making 
tools (2.1) are typically less complicated than the certifications (2.2) which are very complex. The 
earlier in the design process the tools are used, the larger effect they can have. However, the early 
design phases mean many assumptions and uncertainties in the sustainability evaluation. It is 
believed that there is a need for more simplified methods in general12,27,33 and it would be beneficial 
to better coordinate the large number of existing tools and methods for the decision-making.  
 
Objectives and methodology - analysis of the three pillars 
Inspired by the tool Renobuild (2.1.4), several methods were used for the analysis in the proposed 
methodology and case study. It was found that in the sustainable approach, assessment of a product 
==PAGE:59==
50 
 
within its whole life cycle point plays a key role. The integrated approach is also important because 
the most environment-friendly solution does not necessarily have to be the most efficient from the 
economic point of view too.8 
Starting with the most important pillar according to the tenants and society,27 it was said many times 
that the social pillar is difficult to cover and is therefore disregarded in many tools.27,33,53 This is due 
to the qualitative approach used in the assessment of the social development, which is more 
complicated with regards to the data management than the quantitative one used in the other methods. 
The large amount of data necessary for the analysis of the social pillar can be an outcome of forms 
or questionnaires filled in by users with adequate knowledge or by multiple stakeholders in a 
workshop.27 The obstacle related to these data is the difficulty to categorize them and the subjectivity 
related to the quantitative analysis (in order to compare the results between the three pillars, it is 
necessary to quantify the data). There is a need for a better integration of conservation and 
architecture through simplified methods to evaluate these qualities early in the process.33 There are 
ongoing initiatives to further develop the method of evaluating social sustainability.53 
SLCA was found as an alternative bringing the social and environmental pillars together. This 
method follows the same procedure as the environmental LCA but presents the results in a form of 
damage categories related to the social impact. 60 LCA method and underlying data are still being 
developed (for example, the inventory related to transportation or production shows a lack of 
measurements). In reality, LCA would not capture the whole life cycle of the renovation process due 
to the restraints.53 
Generally, there are many assumptions in this method which challenge the credibility of the results. 
As mentioned, several life cycle stages are not included in the LCA.57 For example, the transportation 
of material is neglected, as it was confirmed within the special course related to the master thesis that 
this does not have a large influence on the environmental impact. However, this item can vary a lot 
according to where the material is taken from. Another example could be the level of recycling of 
the material at the end of its life. This is hard to predict but the assumption can have a significant 
impact on the environmental impact of the examined solution alternative.   
Initially, the Danish tool LCAbyg for LCA of buildings was used. The tool leaves some space for 
the program user to insert his own database which makes it suitable for the users with LCA 
experience. As a result, another tool was used in the research instead, due to the lack in the inventory 
database of the LCAbyg. On the other hand, another Danish tool - LCCbyg - which was used for the 
LCCA offers an easy estimation of the total cost across the different life cycle stages. A difference 
between the tools was noticed – both tools approach the end of life of material differently. 
Considering the life span of a material 50 years, LCAbyg would assume replacement of the material 
at the end of year 50. LCCbyg calculates the end of life before year 50 – thus it is included in the 
year 49.  
The two methods LCA and LCCA consider different categorization of the elements. In LCA the 
materials are divided according to the life cycle stages. In LCC, the building elements are categorized 
according to the SfB classification as described in 3.2.1. This brings substantial difficulties in 
connection of the pillars and implementation of the integrated sustainable approach.  
Another challenge is in the study period because the DGNB methodology suggests a 50-year time 
span but in some literature the suggested period for the LCC is shorter.7 This is due to the fact that 
the discounting procedure used in this method decreases the importance of the future costs – the more 
distant in the future, the less important they are in the total LCC (the speed of the diminution is 
==PAGE:60==
51 
 
dependent on the DR and inflation as described in 3.2.2). Discussing the time span, it must also be 
mentioned that the longer the study period is, the less predictable it is due to the uncertainty of future 
development.63 Retrofit and improvement measures taken during the building lifetime would 
improve a building’s state and performance and could influence the results over a long study period 
but are difficult to predict and therefore neglected in the analyses. 
In both of the methodologies, a large amount of data is handled. If the methods are to be utilized in 
the solution alternatives generation and evaluation, the process must be more efficient and less 
time-consuming. Future work on this issue is discussed further later in this chapter. 
 
Results of the analysis – recommendations for building design 
Based on the performed case study LCA and LCCA, several observations and recommendations for 
the building design are presented. These recommendations cannot be the outcome of the case study 
only, because the evaluation might be different from case to case due to many affecting factors such 
as building usage, building envelope, overall setup, used materials aso. Therefore, the statements are 
supported by references. It must also be reminded that the final evaluation and contribution of 
different life cycle phases might differ according to the size (building materials) and usage (energy 
consumption) of the building. 
LCA is sometimes described as evaluation of the building materials and the energy consumption.96 
The energy consumption usually accounts for the major part of the environmental impact with 
heating being the main cause. Second is the building material. With decreasing energy consumption, 
the influence of the materials increases (when dealing with low energy consumption the embodied 
energy in materials used can vary between 9 to 46% and in conventional buildings from 2 to 38%). 
End of life of the materials has a smaller impact.1 The impact can especially be decreased by 
recycling of the building materials.  
With regards to the environmental impact, the design process should always focus on reduction of 
the energy consumption first and then choose construction materials with the same functionality 
having the lowest impact.97 If the concern is to reduce the environmental impact of a building, the 
attention should be focused on reducing the impacts of the walls and roof.98 These elements are also 
important from the energy consumption perspective, where together with windows these surfaces are 
the main structures for the heat transfer between the indoor and outdoor environment.99 Apart from 
the recycling which was already mentioned, the usage of local materials can contribute to the 
reduction of the environmental impact.1 Metals are responsible for a large part, and also wood 
products and gypsum boards can significantly decrease the environmental impact.100  
During the design of green buildings, utilized LCA can be misunderstood and lead to usage of 
renewable resources.1 This brings a risk of people focusing more on using renewable resources than 
the thermal enclosure which is the real essence for making a building efficient. During the building 
design, the passive solution for decreasing the energy consumption should be applied first, followed 
by active saving solutions and renewable resources for the energy as the final step.101 Last but not 
least, it is more cost-effective to invest in energy efficiency than in renewable technologies. 95  
During the energy simulations, it was noticed that only applying the recommended U-values in BR15 
is not enough to meet the requirements for the primary energy consumption of new buildings in 2015. 
The proposal is an outcome of optimization between the satisfactory indoor environment and the 
means how to provide it. Air exchange of the ventilation plays a big role in this optimization as this 
==PAGE:61==
52 
 
ensures the optimum CO2 concentration but also strongly influences the heating demand (higher air 
exchange means higher demand). Other parameters playing a role in this optimization would be the 
building envelope and building thermal capacity. Cooling nowadays receives a big attention due to 
its use in achieving the required thermal comfort but simultaneously increasing the energy 
consumption. 
Discussing the LCC, broader approach to the building is used in this method compared to LCA. It 
takes into account the costs related to acquisition, use and maintenance, renewal and adaptation, and 
disposal.69 The discounting to NPV has such a big influence in Life Cycle Cost Analysis, that costs 
after 40 years bring almost no increase in the total NPV.7 The lower the discount rate, the higher the 
influence over years. The discount rate is dependent on inflation and interest rate which are currently 
historically low.52 
In many cases, the cost of operating a building may be five times higher than the construction costs.7 
So if the operating cost can be reduced, it can pay for the larger increase in construction costs.102 
Same as in LCA, the lower the consumption, the higher the influence of the building material. The 
contribution of heating to the total consumption cost is lower than to the environmental impact caused 
by the use phase. The most significant elements in the case study from the LCC point of view are 
steel and concrete structures, meaning load-bearing elements, floors and external walls,98 also 
representing the majority of mass in the building. 
During the design process, the life cycle of the materials should be kept in mind when designing the 
multiple-layer building structures. The life cycle of an element is determined by the shortest life cycle 
of the material in the element, if the element with the shortest life cycle is not surrounded and 
protected properly by elements with longer LC. This principle, referred to as Six S-s (site, structure, 
skin, services, space plan and stuff) describes that different layers interact with different groups of 
people. The design should follow the systems from the slower to the faster ones with regards to the 
changes and exchanges. The slow systems should not block the flow of the quick ones and oppositely 
the fast ones should not tear up the slow ones 103 ( a typical example is a foil with relatively low LC, 
located in the middle of construction, meaning complicated replacement with regards to the 
surrounding materials).  
At the end of this section, it must be remembered that the results are dependent on the local standards 
(as the proposals mainly aim to meet the requirements in the standards)24 and conditions and it might 
vary a lot in different countries and environments. The recommendations for the design or 
observations based on the case study discussed earlier in the report might not be valid in other than 
mild climates. The ratio of energy consumption and material contribution would be different.24  
 
Obstacles in the methodology, ideas for improvements and future work 
Several comments on the proposed methodology, its obstacles and ideas for improvements are 
described in the next few paragraphs.  
The first and main observation follows the remarks already mentioned earlier about the 
methodologies used in the analysis – LCA and LCCA. The presented results are the outcome of a 
process with several assumptions and simplifications. Considering the fact that the earlier in the 
design process the decision support tools are used, the higher impact they can have (meaning increase 
of the effectivity while not increasing the cost of the solution), and the earlier in the design process 
==PAGE:62==
53 
 
we are, the less detailed the building model is, this obstacle might seem irrelevant. However, the 
detailed analysis could bring some changes in the results. 
This brings us to a general issue in the building analyses – they mean a very time-consuming process 
(100 hours for basic or quick and 200 hours for complete sustainability assessment)46. Within the 
space of solutions, there can be big differences among the solution alternatives which means several 
analyses conducted for various solutions and significant extension of the design process duration. 
BIM and digitalizing of the proposal offers a great solution for increasing the efficiency of the process 
and handling the large amount of data. 
Integration of LCA and LCCA into the model can be for example found in Tally tool104 serving for 
the environmental assessment and Sigma Revit integration105 for the cost estimations. However, these 
tools are integrated separately and do not offer the linked sustainability evaluation. Another solution 
is an open language IFC (Industry Foundation Classes) which is used to exchange and retrieve data 
from the BIM model, working in a way of linking property set and quantity set. All these tools are 
very new and not fully used in the building industry yet. In order to gain the benefits of BIM, the 
level of understanding among partners involved in the same project needs significant improvements. 
Information Delivery Manual is one of the solutions providing a formal and accurate description of 
the processes among actors and tools interacting around a BIM model.35 This is one of the first steps 
in the future to successfully implement the sustainability assessment in the design process. 
How to include the social pillar in this computational assessment leads to another discussion, which 
needs an integrated collaboration of engineers and sociologists. This research covered the indoor 
environment and disregarded many of the social evaluation criteria because they were considered the 
same in the 3 scenarios compared within the case study (such as for example access for users with 
physical impairments). Several issues would, however, work differently across the scenarios and 
influence the results. 
Such an issue is, for example, the influence of the construction works – representing annoyance of 
the users and neighbors. A problem connected to refurbishments is where to accommodate the 
occupants during the construction. During a renovation, the functioning of the building might be 
limited but still working partially in some areas, while during the demolition and replacement, the 
building must be cleared out. The extra space necessary for the storage and accommodation during 
the construction works brings an additional cost, which was not included in the calculations. It should 
also be researched what is the duration difference between the construction works of different 
solutions and related annoyance (noise, vibrations, traffic limitations around the site aso.) Generally, 
the impact of construction works of the solutions would require detailed planning of the construction 
for different alternatives and their further investigation, which was unfortunately not covered within 
this research due to the time limitations. 
 
The imaginary scenario 
As the last point of the discussion, the focus is on the introduced imaginary scenario and the 
methodology, how to evaluate whether it is more sustainable to renovate an existing building or 
demolish it and replace it with a new building.  
It must be reminded that the decision is not only between the two scenarios – deep renovation versus 
demolition and replacement as the project title proposes. The process leading to the best renovation 
solution consists of many alternatives, which were not compared with the replacement scenario in 
==PAGE:63==
54 
 
the presented case study. Based on the statement that it would be beneficial to better coordinate the 
large number of existing tools and methods for the decision-making,33 the scenario was introduced 
into one of the methods which compare these alternatives. The original goal of the research was to 
create a decision-making tree but this idea was dropped out due to the already existing large amount 
of tools and the final product was therefore changed. Another fact playing a role in this decision was 
the enormous amount of data which would have to be handled in the decision-making tree tool. This 
could be possible in the future, when the sustainability evaluation methods are fully digitalized and 
integrated into the design process. It could also be an option to make this evaluation an additional 
point in the sustainability assessment of the final solution. Then this could be done almost 
automatically by a computer software serving and developed for this purpose, especially after 
utilizing the computational methods discussed earlier.  
The model of the imaginary new building should stay as close as possible to the best renovation 
alternative while meeting the requirements of current standards for new constructions. The best 
renovation alternative presents a deep renovation where the materials and energy performance 
changes come into play (compared to only small improvement measures). The existing construction 
might bring a risk of hazardous materials and complicate the renovation process. 
The application of an additional insulation might bring some issues. It was considered that the load 
bearing structures are fixed and positioned as they would be in the renovation case. The additional 
layers were installed on the exterior surface of these elements. This means that the gross floor area 
of the solution with more insulation is larger which with small growth (e.g. < 5%) might be 
acceptable in a self-standing case. In other cases (detached or row houses), this could, however, mean 
decrease in the rentable space and influence the results. The application of exterior insulation would 
also cause small differences in the appearance of the building.  
The new solution would mean different loads to the load-bearing structures and the preliminary static 
calculations should confirm the stability of the construction. In the case study, the executed 
renovation already brought some changes in the loads. After a consultation with a structural engineer, 
it was assumed that this structure would have the capacity to carry the loads in the new building.  
The structural solution itself leads to another discussion. In the case study, a prefabricated structural 
system, which could still be considerable in a new construction nowadays, was used. If this was not 
the case, it is suggested to count a relevant solution using materials similar to the best renovation 
alternative to ensure the comparability of the scenarios. The steel beams, used for strengthening the 
structure around old and newly created holes, would most probably not be installed in a new 
construction. Since they have a large influence in both LCA and LCCA, it was investigated how the 
results would be influenced in the case of concrete structure only. These steel beams were disregarded 
and the amount of concrete was increased by 20%. The total environmental impact of this solution 
across the 4 damage categories is 2-6% lower compared to Sc 3 in the 50-year study period. In the 
manufacturing phase, the difference is 11±5%. The most visible savings occur in the category 
‘Ecosystem quality,’ where the steel shows the largest impact. In the total LCC, the savings for the 
50-years study period are 2%, representing 7% savings in the construction cost.  
In the reality, a new building replacing an old one would use current construction approach, to meet 
the latest functional demands and the flexibility requirements. Consequently, it might result in the 
new building being very different from the renovation case. Through history, there were two main 
streams with different opinions on the preservation approach. One group (purism) claimed that the 
building should functionally and visually become as close to the original, as possible. The other one 
stated that the new buildings should not follow the original design and should reflect on the current 
==PAGE:64==
55 
 
needs and development stage of the society (aesthetic dogmatism).106 The author of this project 
believes in the second opinion. A new building might in general lead to a more sustainable solution 
due to fewer restrictions caused by the already existing construction in the renovation scenario. 
Current market might also offer many construction opportunities with smaller environmental impact 
and cost (as for example investigated in the previous paragraph). The middle-way Sc 2 highlights the 
potential of the energy savings of a new building. It also shows the influence of the existing 
construction. If a building was demolished and replaced with exactly the same building, it would 
only prolong its life span but increase the environmental and financial impact. Through the 
imaginary scenario, this research aimed for questioning and challenging the commonly used 
approaches in the sustainable renovation design. 
The main question is what should happen in the real world if the sustainability assessment results in 
favor of demolition and replacement with a new building? The built environment is an important part 
of Europe’s cultural heritage, an identity identifier, a core for socio-economic development and also 
strongly contributes to the global tourism.33 Due to the sensitivity of the topic, it is proposed to 
conduct several case studies using this methodology and to continue in this research. If the studies 
over the 50-year study period lead to differences between scenarios larger than specific percentage 
(suggested 15% as it was in the presented case study), certain measures should be taken.  
Some studies show that the actual life of buildings exceeds the estimated service life, and that from 
the environmental point of view there is a preference for renovation over demolition.33 The life cycle 
of a building is around 100 years, depending on the building size, usage aso. This long life span 
brings many paradoxes. Looking at a building as a product, how should we predict its functioning 
over the long life span? The users’ needs change, as well as new technologies which could provide 
a better solution to satisfy the upcoming needs. However, the buildings, scarcely designed to satisfy 
the needs over their life time, are used for longer period. Executing the demolition – which might be 
the most sustainable solution at a certain point as shown in the case study – would, on the other hand, 
decrease the building LC. A general decrease of the considered life cycle of the buildings would have 
an impact on the market where the assets present an important item just because of their long life 
span. Massive demolitions would also have an impact on landfilling and taking of land (which was 
not covered in the case study) which then plays in favor of renovation. 
Instead of demolition, the solution would be to increase the energy efficiency of the existing building 
stock during renovating even more, as it was proven that the energy consumption plays the main role 
in the sustainability assessment. The following picture shows the levels of steering. Applying the 
energy requirements to the standards would be the most effective solution.107  
 
Figure 39 Hierarchy of levels of steering. Each level is necessary for reaching the total effect107 
• Policy level strategies, building law, EU directives
• Policy level guidelines and building code development
• Municipal steering strategies and practices
• Steering models of building permit authorities
• Procurement and processes level steering models
• Project level management and leadership
==PAGE:65==
56 
 
The changes in standards would also be a solution to the problem of how to persuade the owners to 
generally involve the sustainability in the renovation projects which are then looking beyond their 
period of interest.24 
At the end, it should be pointed out that the Danish government puts a lot of effort into the 
sustainability of the building sector. Such an example is the sustainability package for the building 
political strategy in Denmark released in 2015, which was utilized in this research. Large amount of 
the reviewed literature and tools were in Danish which brought limitations to the research. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:66==
57 
 
7 CONCLUSION 
The impulse for conducting this research, namely the lack of the involvement of demolition and 
replacement as one of the alternatives included in the building renovation design process, was 
verified. It was found out that various tools for sustainable renovation decision-making are available 
on the market, offering a comparison of different renovation alternatives and sustainability and 
performance assessment of a building project. However, the considerations about the demolition and 
replacement of an existing building as one of the solution alternatives in the methodologies are 
limited.  
Investigating the sustainable renovation decision-making, it was found out that the investors make 
their decisions mainly based on the initial cost and energy savings when discussing the solution to 
the problems evoking the energy renovation, lacking the overall sustainable approach.94 The 
sustainable decision support tools are therefore extremely important during the discussion among 
various stakeholders to make the renovation more sustainable. The earlier in the design process the 
tools are used, the larger effect they can have. However, the early design phases mean many 
assumptions and uncertainties in the evaluation.75 More importantly, there is no common 
understanding of the concept of sustainability.47  
There is a need for more simplified methods in general and it would be beneficial to better coordinate 
the large number of existing tools and methods for the decision-making.33 Based on this statement, a 
new imaginary scenario was introduced into one of the already existing tools – Renobuild – in order 
to evaluate whether it is more sustainable to renovate a building or demolish it and replace it with a 
new one. This methodology is flexible with regards to the tools used for the complicated individual 
assessment of the sustainability pillars (social, environmental and economic sustainability) and 
provides a simple and easy-to-read presentation of the results for the decision makers, which was 
determined to be a crucial property12 of a decision support tool in sustainable renovation decision-
making. With this method, the most sustainable renovation solution can be found and compared to 
the imaginary scenario. In the introduced scenario, the new building replacing the original one meets 
the requirements of current standards while keeping fixed as many parameters, as they are in the best 
renovation solution, as possible.  
The scenarios are evaluated from the environmental (LCA), economic (LCC) and social point of 
view. There is a need for better integration of conservation and architecture through simplified 
methods to evaluate these qualities early in the process.33 There are ongoing initiatives to further 
develop the method of evaluating social sustainability.27 The social pillar was evaluated by the 
quality of the indoor environment in the case study. The remaining parameters were discussed and if 
they were the same across the compared alternatives, they stayed out of the comparison. It was found 
that the assessment of a product within its whole life cycle point plays a key role in the sustainable 
approach. The integrated approach is also important because the most environment-friendly solution 
does not necessarily have to be the most efficient from the economic point of view too.8 SLCA was 
found as an alternative bringing the social and environmental pillars together.53 
There were found several differences between LCA and LCC methods which complicate the 
integrated sustainability assessment. The two methods LCA and LCCA assume different 
categorization of the elements. Two tools designed by the Danish government to assist the sustainable 
design – LCAbyg and LCCbyg – approach the end of life of material differently. Another challenge 
==PAGE:67==
58 
 
is in the study period because the DGNB methodology suggests 50-year time span but in some 
literature the suggested period for LCC is shorter, because the discounting to NPV has such an 
influence that the future costs within the last decade of the 50-year period mean insignificant increase 
in the LCC.7 The lower the DR, the higher the influence over years.52 The longer the study period is, 
the less predictable due to the uncertainty of future development.63 
The analyses consider many assumptions and are not yet fully developed and used in the market. 
Because they are also very time consuming due to a large amount of data being processed, the BIM 
and digitalizing of the proposals are a very important step in the implementation of sustainability 
assessment in the design process. In order to gain the benefits of BIM, the level of understanding 
among partners involved in the same project needs significant improvements.35 
The idea of an imaginary scenario was tested on a case study. The best renovation alternative presents 
a deep renovation where the materials and energy performance changes come into play (compared 
to only small improvement measures). In the case study, the second imaginary scenario was also 
included to examine the processes’ differences. In this case, the existing building was demolished 
and replaced with a building with exactly the same parameters as the reference case of renovation. 
Investigating the different pillars, the scenarios were researched over 30, 50 and 100 years study 
periods. When finally comparing the scenarios among each other, the study period of 50 years was 
used and the results were brought together in the form of a graph designed by the Renobuild 
methodology. According to the results, the demolition and replacement is a more sustainable solution 
than an already executed renovation. 
The analysis pointed out the large influence of the energy performance in both the environmental 
and economic impact. From the environmental perspective, the heating is the main cause of the 
building impact, followed by the building material. The end of life of the materials has a smaller 
impact. The environmental impact can be especially decreased by recycling of the building 
materials.1 In many cases, the cost of operating a building may be five times higher than the 
construction costs.7 So if the operating cost can be reduced, it can pay for the larger increase in 
construction costs.102 The contribution of heating to the total consumption cost is lower than to the 
environmental impact caused by the use phase. The most significant elements in the case study from 
the LCC point of view except the energy consumption are steel and concrete structures, meaning 
load-bearing elements, floors and external walls, also representing the majority of mass in the 
building in the case study. Generally, it can be concluded, that the lower the consumption, the higher 
the influence of the building material.108 
Several recommendations with regards to how to decrease the environmental and economic impact 
of a building design were discussed. During the building design, it should be remembered that the 
passive solution for decreasing the energy consumption should be applied first, followed by active 
savings solutions and renewable resources for the energy as the final step.101 Last but not least, it is 
more cost-effective to invest in energy efficiency than in renewable technologies.95   
Discussing the demolition and replacement of an existing building, it should be said that a new 
building might in general lead to a more sustainable solution due to fewer restrictions caused by the 
already existing construction in the renovation scenario. Often, the initial cost of a renovation is 
increased during the construction due to issues not discovered in the previous survey. The existing 
construction might bring a risk of hazardous materials (paints, sealants, impregnations, adhesives, 
plastics, metals)109 which would complicate the renovation process. Current market might also offer 
many construction opportunities with smaller environmental impact and cost. It might also offer a 
better indoor environment as it was in the case study.  
==PAGE:68==
59 
 
By the imaginary scenario, this method aimed for questioning and challenging the commonly used 
approaches in the sustainable renovation design. It can be concluded that if the time span after the 
intervention is above 30 and the savings between the imaginary and the renovation scenarios were 
high, demolition and rebuilding is the best option. Lower savings and shorter study periods result in 
favor of renovation or transformation.5 Due to the sensitivity of the topic, it is proposed to conduct 
several case studies of different types and sizes of buildings using this methodology and continue in 
this research. If the studies over the 50-year study period lead to the differences between scenarios 
larger than the specific percentage (suggested 15% as it was in the presented case study), certain 
measures should be taken.  
If the buildings were demolished and replaced when this scenario resulted in being more sustainable 
when a deep renovation is considered, it could mean decreasing the life span of buildings and it 
would have an enormous impact on the building industry. This impact should be researched as a next 
step if it was previously confirmed that the demolition and replacement would result in a more 
sustainable solution in many cases. 
Another solution would be to increase the energy efficiency of the existing building stock during 
renovating even more (as it was proven that the energy consumption has the largest influence in the 
sustainability assessment). However, existing buildings can bring some complications with regards 
to the energy efficiency improvement measures. If a renovation brings only small improvements in 
the energy performance of the existing building, it can be expected that the demolition and 
replacement with a new building would bring a more sustainable solution considering the whole 
building life span. The improvements could be done through changes in standards and regulations 
which is leading to the last conclusion. 
The attitude of state plays an important role in the sustainability involvement and in the proposed 
methodology. In Denmark, where this research took place, a lot of effort has been put into sustainable 
buildings and is currently put into the sustainable renovation. This research pointed out that the 
traditional mindset about sustainable renovation might not be correct and needs alterations.

1 INTRODUCTION 
This report summarizes the content of work conducted in a special course ‘Life Cycle 
Management in Buildings’ supervised by Per Anker Jensen at DTU in spring 2016. This special 
course is related to the author’s master thesis ‘Evaluation of deep renovation of an existing 
multi-use building versus its demolition and replacement with a new building’ which has been 
carried out simultaneously and is due in August 2016. The course provides a necessary 
background for the master thesis.  
This paper focuses on theory of life cycle management in buildings and methodologies used for 
buildings analysis. The methods are described and used to analyze a case study – one of three 
scenarios compared in the related master thesis. The results are discussed in order to understand 
the importance of different life cycle stages.  
Building industry counts for around 40% of the total energy consumption in Europe.1 This fact 
is an impulse for improvements of energy efficiency of existing buildings2 and more sustainable 
approach in design and planning of new buildings. Life cycle management is a powerful 
framework assisting the sustainable development of building industry. 
This approach evaluates building from the whole life cycle point of view. For the environmental 
evaluation the method called life cycle assessment is used. The economic efficiency is evaluated 
through life cycle cost. Both methods divide the life cycle into different phases and are often 
presented separately, even though there is a link between them. The methods can compare 
different solution alternatives and help to choose the most sustainable one. Often the variation 
with the lowest LCC is not the variant with the lowest environmental impact. This usually leads 
to an iterative process in the design in order to find the most sustainable solution. 3 
The methods can also be used for showing the importance of different life cycle stages, as it is 
done in this report. 
Challenges and some observations from the implementation of the methods to the case study 
are discussed at the end of the report. 
 
 
 
 
82
==PAGE:159==
4 
 
2 LIFE CYCLE MANAGEMENT 
Life cycle management (LCM) is a powerful framework for investigation and design of 
buildings in order to sustainably develop building industry. Methods like life cycle analysis 
(LCA) and life cycle costing (LCC) are used with focus on the whole life cycle (LC) of a 
building, evaluating environmental and economic aspects respectively.3 Combining these two 
methodologies can lead to more holistic decision making within the building design and 
contribute to creation of effective business strategies.  
In the building design, combination of both methods is more important than in a study of an 
existing building. The methods should be used in very early phases of the project design and 
through an iterative process should bring the most sustainable solution to satisfy the project 
requirements. 
The economic and environmental evaluation are together with social aspect three basic pillars 
for sustainable development. The social aspect among others sets the requirements for the 
product, which can be provided by solution with different environmental and economic 
properties. This aspect is hard to determine through the whole life cycle as the needs of the 
users change according to the society development which is not easily predictable. The 
evaluation of social aspect is not yet fully covered with a specific methodology but is part of 
some sustainability evaluation tools. 4,5 
In general, the evaluation within the whole life cycle is based on many assumptions and 
predictions. Detailing of the analyses brings often too much time-consuming procedure. 
Assessment from life cycle (LC) is key point in sustainable development and assessment. Life 
cycle is divided into different phases in the two methodologies. 
 In LCA the phases ‘manufacturing,’ ‘construction,’ ‘use’ and ‘end of life’ are considered. 6 
 In LCC the phases are ‘acquisition,’ ‘use and maintenance,’ ‘renewal and adaptation’ and 
‘disposal.’7 
When investigating a case or solution alternative, the methods can be used more or less 
separately but the person making the analyses should still keep in mind their connection. Some 
tools like BEES or LEGEP already combine the LCA and LCC in one procedure. 
There should always be a reference case in the analyses interpretation in order to determine the 
effectiveness of the solution. The methods can also analyze a self-standing case for 
understanding the influence of the different life cycle stages or determining further strategies. 
This is the case of the presented study in this report. 
 
 
 
 
83
==PAGE:160==
5 
 
3 LIFE CYCLE ANALYSIS (LCA) 
Life cycle analysis, also called life cycle assessment, is a tool that provides building 
professionals with understanding of the environmental impact of the energy consumption and 
other factors occurring through all the life cycle phases of a building. LCA methods used in the 
building construction industry are mainly based on process-based LCA.3 This method uses 
inputs (materials and energy resources) and outputs (impact or damage parameters described 
later in 3.1.3) for each step of a product production. This procedure enables evaluation of 
potential environmental impacts associated with those inputs and outputs. 
Table 1 provides a brief overview of types of process-based LCA methods. This paper focuses 
on cradle-to-grave type. 
Cradle-to-Grave 
Cradle-to-Gate 
Cradle-to-Cradle

cycle 
Specific type of 
Cradle-to-grave

End of life disposal

To use and disposal

Table 1 Variations of process-based LCA methods 3 
It has been agreed that the ISO 14040 family of LCA standards should be used as a starting 
point for further development of LCA methodology within the building industry sector. 8 
 
Steps of the LCA Process 
LCA consist of 4 steps according 
to EN ISO 14040:  
 Goal and Scope Definition,  
 Inventory Analysis,  
 Impact Assessment  
 Interpretation.  
 
LCA is an iterative technique and 
as data and information are 
collected, various aspects of the 
scope may require modification 
in order to meet the original goal 
of the study.3

3.1.1 Goal and Scope Definition 
According to EN ISO 14040 “the goal of an LCA states the intended application, the reasons 
for carrying out the study and the intended audience. The scope includes among others the 
product system to be studied, the functional unit, the system boundary, selected impact 
categories and methodology of impact assessment, assumption and limitations.” The functional 
unit offers a reference necessary to ensure comparability of LCA results. The inputs and outputs 
are related to the functional unit. The breadth and depth of the proposed LCA is specified by 
the system boundary.9 
In building industry many LCA studies follow the DGNB International simplified 
methodology. This methodology is part of DGNB certification system serving for sustainability 
assessment of buildings. System boundaries and life cycle phases are described by following 
figure.    
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 2 System boundaries defined in DGNB International 6 
85
==PAGE:162==
7 
 
3.1.2 Inventory Analysis 
This step involves collection of data and calculations quantifying relevant inputs (energy, raw 
material, ancillary and other physical inputs) and outputs (emissions to air, discharges to water 
and soil and other environmental aspects) of a product system. 
Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) is a crucial element for this step and needs a big attention. The 
inventory analysis requires software tools with large focus on databases. The tool varies from 
simplest software tools in form of spreadsheets to more complex tools acting more like cost-
estimating software.3 Here follows a list of some LCA tools.

3.1.3 Impact Assessment 
In this step the emissions from process or product are translated into impacts on various human 
and terrestrial eco-systems, presented as impact categories defined in scoping. The results from 
this step can either be presented by a single value obtained by applying weights or different 
impact categories separately. 3  
Impact Categories – Big 6 
Impact categories transform the quantities of emissions into a contribution of a product to a 
specific environmental problem. Here follows a list of impact categories also known as Big 6, 
11 which is covered by LCAbyg.  
 Global Warming Potential (GWP) 
Global Warming Potential documents the increase of temperature in the lower 
atmosphere to a level above normal. This is caused by the content of carbon dioxide 
(CO2) and other ‘greenhouse’ gasses. 12 The unit of measurement is kg CO2 per 
functional unit (FU) or product.  
 
 Ozone Depletion Potential (ODP) 
Ozone Depletion Potential measures the impact on thinning of the ozone layer. The unit 
is R-11 per FU or product.  
 
 Photochemical Ozone Creation Potential (POCP) 
The POCP (also known as summer smog) is defined as the amount of low level ozone 
and the initiators are NOx and volatile organic compounds coming in contact with 
sunlight. The unit of measurement is kg Ethane per FU or product. 
 
 Acidification Potential (AP) 
Acidification means the absorbance of acid gases (principally nitrogen and sulfur 
compounds) by ecosystems through dissolution in rain. The unit of measurement is kg 
SO2 per FU or product. 
 
 Eutrophication Potential (EP) 
Eutrophication causes decrease of species in ecosystems caused by the absorbance of 
mineral nutrients into the soil or water. The unit of measurement is kg Phosphate per 
FU or product. 
 
 Primary Energy (PE) 
Primary energy refers to an energy form found in the nature without any conversion or 
transformation process. The unit of measurement is kWh per FU or product. 3 
 
87
==PAGE:164==
9 
 
Damage and midpoint categories – IMPACT 2002+ 
The database in tool LCAbyg is very limited and therefore tool Quantis Suite 2.0 was used 
instead which works with the most famous LCA database – ecoinvent. 13 Quantis Suite 2.0 
follows IMPACT 2002+ methodology showing 1 midpoint category and 4 damage categories 
as follows. 
 
Figure 3 Overall scheme of the IMPACT 2002+ vQ 2.2 framework linking LCI results via the midpoint categories to 
damage categories, based on Jolliet et al. 14 15 
 
 Human Health [DALY]  
The “human health” damage category is the sum of 5 top midpoint categories as can be 
seen in Figure 3 and is expressed in DALYs. “Disability-Adjusted Life Years” 
characterizes the disease severity, accounting for both mortality and morbidity.  The 
human health average damage is 0.0071 DALY/point. 14 
 
 Ecosystem quality [PDF.m2.y] 
“PDF·m2·y” (“Potentially Disappeared Fraction of species over a certain amount of m2 
during a certain amount of year”) is the unit to measure the impacts on ecosystems. The 
PDF·m2·y represents the 3 years of life lost distributed over the overall population and 
NOT per person. 14 
 
 Climate change [kg CO2-eq] 
Climate change can be also presented as global warming with units “kg equivalent of a 
reference substance s.” It expresses the amount of a reference substance s that equals 
the impact of the considered pollutant within the midpoint category studies (e.g., the 
88
==PAGE:165==
10 
 
Global Warming Potential on a 100-y scale of fossil based methane is 27.75 times higher 
than CO2, thus its CF is 27.75 kg CO2-eq). 14 
 
 Resources [MJ] 
Resulting from “non-renewable energy” and “mineral extraction”, this category 
measures the amount of energy needed to extract the resource or extracted. 14 
 
 Water withdrawal [m3] 
The only midpoint category “water withdrawal” shows the water use expressed in m3 
of water needed (consumed, evaporated or spent differently in related processes, without 
water turbined.) 14 
 
3.1.4 Interpretation 
LCA results are reported in the most informative way and serve for systematic evaluation of 
needs and opportunities to reduce the environmental impact of the products or services. Tables 
and graphs are used for the result presentation in order to directly support environmentally 
friendly decision making. In case of planning or design, this step can be the impulse for 
iteration. 9 
 
 
89
==PAGE:166==
11 
 
4 LIFE CYCLE COST 
Life cycle cost (LCC) presents an economic assessment of an item, area, system, or facility, 
expressed in term of equivalent currency. It is considering all the significant costs of ownership 
over its economic life.16 
Life-cycle cost analysis (LCCA) is a method used for assessing the total cost of facility 
ownership. LCCA is especially useful in comparison of different project alternatives, fulfilling 
the same performance requirements but differing in the initial and operating costs.17 Many 
investors make decisions based on initial capital outlay cost, which is only a portion of the costs 
over an asset’s LC. The total cost of ownership of an asset is often far greater than the initial 
capital outlay cost and can vary significantly between different alternative solutions to a given 
operational need.18  
There are several economic evaluation methods. This report focuses on lowest LCC which is 
the most straightforward and easy to interpret one.17 Consistent classification is necessary for 
comparability of different alternatives. EN 15686-6 terminology was perceived as inconsistent 
and the cost classification is far from practical experiences in Nordic countries.19 Several 
classification systems are applied according to local needs and habits. As the Danish tool for 
LCCA called LCCbyg is used, the classification used in this software is explained in following 
chapter. This tool was created together with LCAbyg in spring 2015 as part of sustainable 
package for the building political strategy.  
 
LCC Classification 
As a result of joint Nordic project the main services were defined as can be seen in Table 3. 
The classification is based on SfB classification. The experience indicates that a general account 
plan should be very generic so it can be adapted to the individual building owner. 20 In the 
template for project design, the costs are divided into: 
 Consulting, programming and builder 
 Building elements 
 Fixtures and equipments 
 Management 
 Supplies 
 Cleaning 
90
==PAGE:167==
12 
 
 
Table 3 Main cost categories and their description19 
In EN ISO 15686-5, the cost categories are divided into 4 phases: 
 Acquisition 
 Use and maintenance 
 Renewal and adaptation 
 and Disposal. 7 
 
Terms and Basic Principals in LCC 
4.2.1 Net Present Value 
LCC allows to compare different variations for a proposal through net present value. 
In order to compare the selected alternatives for a solution, the cost estimates over calculation 
period must be recalculated to a single time, usually the start point. This process is called 
discounting the future costs to a present value. The value today of money paid in the future 
is usually lower than the value of money paid today.21 To determine the value today of money 
paid in future, a so-called costing or discount rate is used, which is an expression of expected 
inflation, interest and future risk. 
4.2.2 Inflation and Escalation Rate 
Inflation is a constant rise in the price level caused by growth in the volume of money and credit 
relative to available goods. In the last 25 years the inflation has been stable around 2%.21  
91
==PAGE:168==
13 
 
Figure 4 The present value of a DKK over time at a discount rate 2, 4 and 6%21 
The analysis calculates with constant currency, also referred to as real or deflated currency. In 
order to obtain this value, the escalated value at the inflation rate is discounted to the beginning 
of the project. Escalation rate expresses change in the price caused by combination of 
supply/demand, inflation and other effects.22 
4.2.3 Interest and Discount Rate 
Interest rate determines how many % of certain amount of money is added to get this amount 
of money. This percentage also reflects the risk of getting a loan paid back.23  
Discount rate is coming from the interest rate. There are two types of discount rate: 
1. Real discount rate, which is excluding the inflation rate and is the growth of 
purchasing power. 
2. Nominal discount rate (also called the net rent) is calculated with inflation and 
presents growth rate of money.21 
For the real discount rate the standard prices are used, compared to the nominal discount rate 
which should be used with current prices.24 
As can be seen on following figure, the higher the costing or the discount rate is, the less the 
value of the money paid in the future is today. Therefore a sensitivity analysis of the discount 
rate is investigated.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
4.2.4 Formulas Used for Calculation of NPV 
Present value of the future cost is calculated based on following Eq. 1.25

4.2.5 Lowest Life Cycle Cost 
Lowest LCC is used for determining the cost effectiveness over certain study period or whole 
life cycle and enables comparison of different solution alternatives. The 2 alternatives in Figure 
5, show that profitability of the solutions depends on the time and can change the final proposal 
after analyzing the whole calculation period. 
 
Figure 5 Net present value for two alternatives through 50-year study period 
4.2.6 Salvage value 
Salvage value is also known as residual value and presents the estimated value of an asset at 
the end of its useful life. This cost can have large importance if one alternative requires a 
significant replacement toward the end of its life. 16,26 Salvage value was not considered in the 
case study in this report but will be investigated further in the related master thesis.

5 CASE STUDY 
 
Case Description 
The described methods are now applied to a chosen case study. The object for the investigation 
is Care Center Midtpunkt Farum (PMF). PMF is located in blocks 12 and 13 in Midtpunkt 
Farum – area of 40 blocks in total from 1970s. These two terraced blocks were renovated in 
2014 as a result of cooperation between the municipality and housing association. The 
municipality was looking for space for a new care center and the housing association needed 
some middle-sized apartments which had not been part of the original design and became more 
demanded nowadays. 
 
Figure 6 Care Center Midtpunkt Farum 
This study only covers block 12 with total gross floor area of 7676 m2 which contains 85 
apartments of different sizes and part of the care center. The rest of the care center is positioned 
in adjacent block 13, which is connected to the block 12 via a bridge. The block consists of 4 
floors with parking in the ground floor, housing in the top floor and care center in the rest of 
the building. The building is 140 m long and 31 m wide, longer façades face east and west. 
Within the renovation, the building envelope was improved as can be seen in Table 4.  The 
building was stripped down to the raw concrete and the removed layers were replaced by layers 
with larger thermal resistance. The roof was kept as original.  
Type of construction

Table 4 U values of constructions before and after renovation

==PAGE:171==

Some of the skylights were removed. In the third floor they were replaced by non-transparent 
construction and additional windows were installed on the eastern facade to lighten the corridor. 
Two wings in the 1st floor were removed to enlarge the terraces. All non-load bearing internal 
walls were demolished and the layout was changed as can be seen on following pictures. 
Original staircases were torn down and 4 new were built up connecting all 4 floors because of 
the fire safety requirements. 2 elevators were installed as well. Steel beams were installed to 
ensure the stability of the construction. 
The plumbing was exchanged according to the new layout. Before renovation there were only 
exhausts from the bathrooms in the apartments. During the renovation all apartments were 
equipped with small local ventilation units with heat recovery. The remaining spaces are 
connected to 2 central AHUs located in the ground floor. The new building is heated by 
radiators same as before the renovation.

BIM model and scanned original drawings together with some necessary information were 
provided by NIRAS – the consultant engineers for the renovation. These documents served as 
input for the investigation. 
 
Assumptions for Analysis 
Energy and Water Consumption 
Due to lack of information on the energy consumption of the building after renovation, the 
energy performance was simulated in IDA Indoor Climate and Energy tool. Assumptions and 
input for the simulation are listed in Appendix 1. The new multi-use building was divided into 
institution area and dwelling area and the floors were simulated separately. Resulting energy 
consumption is 56.86 kWh/m2a for the institution part and 51.75 kWh/m2a for the residential 
part. Lighting is assumed to be 3.3 kWh/m2a. 27 This case study is 1 of 3 scenarios compared in 
the related master thesis. Electricity consumption caused by user’s equipment together with 
water consumption are expected to be the same in all three cases and therefore are not included 
in the analysis.     
Presented results are an intermediate step to understand the methodologies and the influence of 
the different life cycle stages. The results in this report might be different from the final results 
presented in the related master thesis.  
The building is investigated within 30, 50 and 100 years long life span. It is considered that the 
renovation prolongs the life span of the building and the building is demolished 100 years after 
the renovation. No changes in the building layout are expected with the 100-year period with 
the awareness of this most probably not being the realistic future scenario. 
The goal of the study is to evaluate the overall environmental impact of renovation and 
following operation of building 12 to help in identifying the life-cycle stages and assemblies 
causing maximum impact in different study periods. The renovated building in 3 different study 
period present 3 functional units. The system boundary is following the description in 3.1.1 
with a few exceptions described in following chapter. 
97
==PAGE:174==
19 
 
 
LCA Assumptions and Results 
The process of the analysis is described on Figure 12.  
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 12 Process of LCA 
Software QS 2.0 offers large flexibility in the inputs supported by the Ecoinvent 2.2 inventory 
database. Compared to LCAbyg which includes life spans of the materials and automatically 
replaces the materials at the end of their life span, the life span is put manually in this program. 
The materials with shorter life span than study period are replaced at the end of their life span 
and steps 2a, 3 and 4 are repeated for the specific materials. If the life span equals to the study 
period, the end of life of the material is considered. 
BIM model in REVIT offers automatic materials take off in m3. Ecoinvent 2.2 database works 
mostly with materials in kg and therefore all the data from the BIM model had to be 
recalculated. 28   
Transport in manufacturing phase is included in the Ecoinvent 2.2 inventory database. 
According to Morten Birkved “The energy related to manufacturing of building elements is 
usually an estimate and there is lack of measurements in this section.” Therefore this energy is 
not included in the case LCA. Transportation from a local storage to the site and from the site 
to an incineration plant was considered in order to explore the environmental impact of this 
transportation within the whole LCA.  
According to Figure 2 the heating, cooling and ventilation units should be part of the LCA. As 
part of the master thesis it is assumed that all the 3 scenarios have the same supply system and 
therefore this is excluded in the LCA.   
The forecasting of development of energy production was simplified29 – the energy production 
is assumed to be the same through the whole study period. 
Recyclable materials representing the major part of building elements are assumed to be 
recycled because recycling of the building materials is essential in decreasing the environmental 
impact of buildings.1 Rest of the materials goes to municipal incineration plant directly and 
landfilling is considered as the last option.

Figure 16 Environmental impact of different life cycle stages – 100 years study period 
 
As can be seen in Figure 13, when considering the environmental impact caused in 100 years 
as 100%, the impact related to material life cycle is maximum 5% in different study periods 
(around 5% deviation from 30 and 50% in the shorter periods.) Furthermore if we continue 
into deeper analyses presented on Figures 14, 15 and 16, it can be seen that the impact related 
to materials decreases with time. The environmental impact caused by use phase varies from 
55% to 85% across the 5 observed impact categories. 
The impact of exchanged materials during the use phase grows significantly from the 30 years 
to the 50 years study period. This is because many materials have the end of their life at 40 
years. Included transport has minor influence and therefore will not be considered in further 
research as recommended in DGNB international. 
The demolition in 100 years from starting point has smaller influence than the impact caused 
by building operation (see Figure 16).

Materials take-off from the BIM model had to be converted to m2 in most of the cases as the 
V&S Pricebooks (commonly used for cost estimates in Denmark) mainly work with these units. 
Price book ‘Bygningsren. Husbygning 2015 brutto’ was used for this case study.  
Cost of technical services was estimated based on value/m2 for different systems in V&S Price 
books. Demolished technical services were not included as these are expected to be the same 
in the three scenarios in master thesis, except the heating elements. Following costs were not 
included in the calculation as these are expected to be the same in all the 3 scenarios investigated 
in the master thesis. 
 Furniture and equipment 
 Management (Taxes, insurance, administration) 
 Energy and water for user’s consumption 
 Cleaning 
Capital Cost 
Total cost of construction is 56.316.779 DKK. 
Administration and contingencies were estimated as percentage of this amount. This estimation 
will be investigated further in the research.

Operating, Maintenance and Renewal Cost 
Maintenance of building elements is annually calculated as percentage of the initial cost of the 
building element.30 It is discounted according to Eq.1 and Eq.2 and all the years are summed 
together for the whole study period.  
Replacement of building elements occurs automatically and is discounted according to Eq,1. 
However, there is a small difference from the LCA calculation. If an element has life cycle 30 
years, the replacement would be counted in only if the study period is >30.  
Prices for energy supply are used as predefined in the LCCbyg template21. 
Disposal cost 
Demolition at the end of 100 years of the building was calculated based on V&S Price books 
and discounted to NPV resulting in 64.712 DKK 
Price Development and Discount Rate 
LCCbyg template suggests price development for different cost groups according to Danish 
Statistics. Price development takes into account inflation of costs. 20 Nominal discount rate 
which is used in the calculation can be set to stable value or steps development can be used. As 
101
==PAGE:178==
23 
 
the discount rate and price development have the largest influence in LCCA,21 sensitivity 
analysis of discount rate is carried out. 
Recommended stable value is 5.5% which is increased and decreased of 2.75% (50% of the 
value). Step DR, which should be used for public buildings is considered to be 4 % for first 36 
years, then is decreased to 3% and from year 71 it is 2%.  
  
Figure 18 Discount rate analysis 
Figure 18 shows that the lower the discount rate is, the higher the influence of the results with 
regards to the calculation period. This is coming from the Eq.1. Taking the 5.5% as the reference 
value, 50% decrease in DR means increase of 30, 62 and 170% LCC in 30, 50 and 100 years 
respectively. 50% increase DR means for the 3 study periods decrease of 29, 39 and 40% 
(reversed values 22, 40 and 62%). So the higher the DR is, the smaller the influence in future 
is. 
Resulting LCC with step DR is between 2.75 and 5% values for 30 and 50-year period. From 
50 to 100 years the value grows almost 4times and presents the highest total LCC in the analysis. 
This method puts large influence on the costs in distant future.

Figure 19 and following figures show more detailed analysis of the DR influence with respect 
to different phases. Cost related to energy supply shows the major part of the total LCC (also 
the largest influence in LCA). In year 30 it varies from 57 to 71%, in year 50 from 72 to 85%. 
In 100 years the influence grows to 84 to 95%. Difference between operating cost from one DR 
to the higher one decreases with time. This is due to the decreasing value of the money paid in 
the future with higher discount rate as shown in Figure 4. 
The decreasing influence of capital cost with time is caused by the capital cost presenting the 
NPV and not being discounted with growing study period. The maintenance cost impact grows 
from 30 to 50 years due to replacement of many materials with life cycle of 40 years.

6 DISCUSSION 
LCA and LCC are both very complex assessment tools, handling large amount of data. As the 
building integrated modelling becomes more popular in the design process, the data serving as 
required input can be taken from the computer models. Integration of LCA and LCCA into the 
model would make the iterative sustainable design process much more time efficient. Some 
efforts of such an integration can be for example registered in Tally tool31 serving for the 
environmental assessment and Sigma Revit integration32 for the cost estimations. These tools 
are however integrated separately and do not offer the linked impact evaluation. 
As mentioned earlier the two methods can be used more or less separately which can cause the 
loss of link between the two assessments. This is also caused by different definition of the life 
cycle phases for each method. As some tools (e.g. BEES, LEGEP) already offer the linked 
assessment, it is proposed to look for inspiration in these tools in further development of the 
methods. However, the results should be still presented for the different aspects as it is believed 
that separated results are easily interpreted and well understood.3 
Both methods are dependent on the location of the project. For example the environmental 
impact of space heating in a building will be different from country to country because of 
different sources for the energy supply. Another example can be found in the prices for 
consultants and architects which cannot be unified internationally.  
Therefore the utilization of the methods strongly depends on the government attitude to 
sustainability. In Denmark, there is a large effort put into the sustainable development of 
building industry set by the Danish building and transport agency. As a result of this effort the 
sustainability package was launched in spring 2015 containing general guidance on sustainable 
construction, two guides on LCA and LCC and basic report on the building’s life cycle. Tools 
for LCA and LCC evaluation were developed as port of this as well.20 
Tool LCCbyg offers many preconfigured settings20, making the LCCA much faster. For 
example the life spans of different building elements are integrated in the program and the 
elements are automatically replaced at the end of their life span. The lifespans for specific 
element varies in both tools and it is proposed to unify these lifespans. Both tools also use 
different units for input of building elements which complicated the total scenario analysis. 
The database of the tool for environmental assessment LCAbyg offers final building elements 
containing all inventory items and also significantly simplifies the LCA process. Unfortunately, 
the database is very limited and this fact led to use of another software at the end. The inventory 
databases in LCA are constantly being expanded. However, many measurements are still 
missing (for example the energy of the production of a building element in the manufacturing 
phase).  
The analyses contain many assumptions (for example the level of recycling of the building 
materials). This can be limited with further research on the analyses input but will bring growth 
of the input complexity. 
105
==PAGE:182==
27 
 
7 CONCLUSION 
Two main methodologies used in life cycle management in buildings were described together with 
the importance of evaluation of the buildings from the whole life cycle point of view. Life cycle 
analysis and life cycle cost were described and used for analysis of the case study. Based on this 
case study and usage of the methods, investigations and recommendations for further research and 
improvements are discussed. 
The case study showed the importance of the use phase increasing with the length of study period.  
The largest focus in sustainable building design from the environmental point of view should 
therefore be put on the energy efficiency and consumption of the buildings (where the wall and roof 
should get the largest attention33). Afterwards the materials with the same functionality but smaller 
environmental impact should be chosen. Implementation of renewable resources like heat pump or 
PV panels should be considered as the third step and not before the previous steps are discussed1.  
In the life cycle cost, the use phase also showed the largest impact. Space heating typically causes 
the largest partial cost. 1 With decreased energy consumption, the influence of construction 
increases. The most important factors in the LCC are discount rate and price developments. The 
sensitivity analysis of discount rate was conducted while keeping the price developments of the cost 
groups at recommended values. The lower the discount rate is, the higher the influence of the results 
with regards to the calculation period. Three times higher discount rate shows four times lower total 
LCC in 100-year study period. Stepped discount rate (used for public buildings) puts large emphasis 
on future costs. The influence of the operating cost grows up to 95% in the 100 year study period. 
The replacement of materials present maximum 2% of the total LCC in this period. Other important 
factors in priority order are lifetimes and restoration percentage followed by operation and 
maintenance data for the specific solution.21  
The demolition of the building at the end of 100-year study period was discounted to NPV. 
Resulting value was insignificant item in the whole LCC and could be neglected. Projected costs 
after 40 years become insignificant by the discounting process. 
The methods serve for comparison of sustainability of different solutions. The tools for LCA and 
LCC mostly work separately and this is a large gap for improvements. When bringing the 
methodologies together, the results should still be presented separately. It is believed that separate 
presentation is more clear and easier to understand than only 1 value obtained by weighed summing 
of different results together3. The earlier in the design stages the methods are used, the larger savings 
they can bring. 16  
Another weakness of the analyses is the uncertainty of future and predicting. Many assumptions are 
part of the analyses which can always lead to discussions. 
The results of analyses should preferably be presented with a reference case in order to be 
comparable.3 This was not the case in this case study where another purpose of the analyses was 
utilized – to determine the impact of different life cycle phases. The main purpose of the analyses 
serving for comparison of different solution alternatives will be used in the related master thesis. 
The final results of the case study presented in the master thesis might be changed from the results 
presented in this report.
